So, what is IIS? Infrastructure as Code (ISC) is the ability to provision and support your computing infrastructure using code instead of manual processes and settings.

How many of you have used IIS before? Any better experience? No? Okay, so what about AWS basic AWS reviews?

So, how do you usually do it? You go to the data appears to log into the credentials, you're launching these two instances, click on that button, and then specify whatever you need. Instead of that one line of code, and you have everything in it. That's what we're going to look at today! It automates infrastructure management, so developers can focus on building and improving applications instead of managing environments. That's a big deal!

Because when you're talking about launching a specific instance, you have so many configurations to go through. Some are optional, some are network-based. All of that. You don't have to work on it as much, and you can just work on the functionality of your app rather than managing your environment.

So, what's wrong with manual infrastructure? It's very time-consuming and prone to error, especially when you manage applications at scale.

What IIS lets you do is define your infrastructure and their desired state, without including all the steps to get to the state.

What are the benefits of this? What is the traditional way of utilizing resources? System admins have a specific role for that. They use a combination of scripts or manual processes to set up infrastructure environments. That is very complex and time-consuming.

The key goal across any computing environment that you don't want manual intervention; you want everything to be automated! That's exactly what IIS uses; it's automating infrastructure to create environments.

Most common use of IIS is in software development to build, test, and deploy applications. So, let's say you have your three environments: You have your test, Dev, and Prod. You don't want to spend time configuring resources. Let's say you have 20 resources, you have like a 10 EC2s or RDS or databases. You want to build your app, but if you want to test it in a different environment, you have to make all those resources from scratch, making sure the configurations of all of those resources are the same. You can't have different configurations, so that is the very common use of IIS where you can just run the Terraform or the CloudFormation file to get your environment set up.

One more benefit of this is obviously you want to make sure you are iterating on best practice environments. Source control allows software developers to easily build and branch on environments.

So, let's say you have a basic IoT code that has like two EC2 instances, but let's say you want to extend it. All you have to do is add that extension block. We can look at what Terraform offers. It offers something known as a state file, so as soon as you run that file and two EC2 instances are made, it keeps track of that, so it doesn't clear them again and again. What all it does is your new block of code that you've added to your code only that is going to execute. Reduce configuration errors!

When you're working with cloud configuration errors are very prone, especially if you don't set up your services properly in the initial phase. So, obviously, anything that involves human developers is error-prone, so people make mistakes; it's fine. There could be configuration drift due to changes in one setup that was missed in another setup, and then IAC also reduces errors and streamlines error checking.

So, for this part where there could be a configuration drift due to changes in one setup, it's exactly like Git. How Git manages your code, you can roll back to the previous table version of it. Let's say we create two subnets: a public subnet and a private subnet. A public subnet is used whenever you have something else customer-based, like hosting a website or computer servers that are public-based and that you can access. But what would you use a private subnet for? Where no one in the world can have access to it, but only the web server that's in your public subnet can have access to it. Any of these cases you can think of any use case. Yes, all your databases go on your private subnet because you don't want all the data even though they can't access it without the IP, you don't want it to be vulnerable. It's completely isolated in the private subnet, and only this web server can get it. You can define some rules such that let's say you have an admin outside the VPC that they can also access it, but that is a different thing. But just understand the basics of VPC, public subnet, private subnet.

Because as a student, you never call lambda function a million times, even a thousand times is very hard for you guys to call it, right? But for industry like Amazon, 24/7, every single second there are so many frames that you can imagine how many. So they found that every single month their cost for using the lambda function and step function is about $400,000. They feel it's too much. So they decided to change the architecture of their application. They discarded the step function. They discarded the surplus. They used EC2 instances because EC2 instances are charged based on hourly rate. But our EC2 instance, say, for a full day, for 24 hours, every hour, say if it's 5 hours for the whole day, one EC2 only costs just over $100. They can use a lot of EC2s, but they can very easily estimate how many EC2s we're going to use and then they can estimate how much money we pay for a month.

So there are a lot of problems managing IAAS by itself. Now let's look at IAAS. The Platform of Private Service.

So IAAS we just learned is it for a daily personal user like our secretary or our sales person, or is it for developers and system engineers? You need to have the expertise. We need to know which service is which. That's one. TaaS is right for developers. When we develop our applications, we would think if there is a use case for a service that I don't want to manage the virtual machines, but as you want a kind of machine that scales by itself, and I specify what runtime I want, and then I can start developing my applications. I want to start early as soon as possible, but without that much work managing the underlying infrastructure. That's PaaS platform as a service.

So it represents a predefined, ready-to-use environment, typically comprised of already deployed and configured IT resources. Why would cloud consumers, for example, a software developer, choose this model to extend on-premise environments into the cloud for scalability and economic purposes?

Though I'm developing my application on on-premise, but I found that as our team is getting bigger and bigger, and we all use the same kind of environment, our server, single server in our office is not enough. You can move to PaaS on the cloud because it scales by itself, and your whole team can be onboarded to the same environment. If you keep adding more new team members, but your environment doesn't change, the platform will automatically scale by itself as it sees more requirements from your team. It's very good for teamwork to develop the same application.

That took entirely substitutes on-premise environment. It can completely replace your environment to become a cloud service provider and deploy their own cloud services to be made a little bit different from consumers. Okay, it's like a tongue twister, but what it means is not like a you can use some existing services, for example, your company develops your own application on IAAS on virtual machine because you have expertise. You develop an application, you present this application to your clients to use, and your application is a PaaS for your clients.

So this means that PaaS doesn't have to be provided by cloud providers; it can be provided by cloud consumers. Trilu is a cloud consumer. This company developed their own application; their application is used by other cloud by other developers to develop their application using their platform. They provide a lot of existing services or tools on their platform for you to develop yours on top of theirs. You get it? PaaS is quite flexible; you can design your own PaaS as well.

Type one virtualization is directly on the hardware. Hypervisor is installed on top of the hardware. What does it mean? Just now we said our virtualization nowadays is not pure software virtualization, it's software-enabled and hardware-accelerated.
So for the same task, if we can make it work using software and also using hardware, which one is faster? Software or hardware? Hardware is faster, right? Of course we want to use hardware to do the virtualization work, right? So we try to use as little software as possible and we try to use as much hardware as possible.
That's why there is a hardware that is special and can enable the virtualization to work or a special kind of hardware and works with a very thin layer of software to virtualize the virtual machines directly from the hardware. This makes the virtualization process very fast! This is a preferred way for big companies or enterprises, but for this one is more expensive and needs more techniques, right? But for personal use, we can use software-enabled or pure software.
So for the first one, we also give it a name: bare metal architecture or bare metal virtualization. For the second way, the hypervisor is on top of an operating system because it's on top of the host software. So the virtualization needs to talk to the host OS and then talk to the hardware. There is one more layer between the hardware and the hypervisor. Of course, the virtualization process is longer, right? It's slower, but it's easier to use for normal users.
Okay, now let's see the differences between them.
First one: The bare metal one. This option represents the installation of a virtualization software directly on the physical host hardware to bypass the host operating system, so there isn't an operating system involved during the virtualization process. However, you do need an operating system, so you do need a license, right? If you need to virtualize your virtual machine on top of some operating system, you need the license for that OS first.
The pros include:
- It sheds the IT resource consumption and the license fees of the host OS
- It's more efficient by interacting with hardware directly
The cons is that compatibility with the hardware devices is an issue. If we want the hardware of the machine to accelerate the hypervisor's work, the hardware should be different, right? The compatibility is an issue. That's why it's mostly used by big companies.
For the second one: the hosted one, which is where the hypervisor is on top of the host OS. We install the virtualization software in a pre-existing OS which is called post-operating system. The pros include:
- Backup and recovery are easier
- It's easier to integrate with directory services

Okay, before we hop on to Docker, let's talk about containerization, which is the main concept behind which we're covering right now.

Firstly, a container is nothing but a standard unit of software that packages up code and all its dependencies. That's exactly how it got its name. You see the containers on a ship. You see how each container is independent, body and phone, containing all its code and dependencies or the environment variables it needs. This essentially does it lets your application run quickly from one computing environment to another.

How many of you work with VMs here? Yeah, so the difference between VMs and containers is VMs have their own dedicated OS, their own kernel. It's completely independent from your host OS. Containers are similar in the way that it is isolated to some extent, but they don't need their own kernel or OS. This will run completely independent and isolated from each other, but it's very lightweight and it doesn't need as much setup as VMs.

Obviously, talking about the security part, like I said, each container is isolated, read-only sandbox. Its contents remain fixed in the state you define. So, let's say we're talking about content in the ship. If you open whatever you put inside the container, it stays the same until you open it up. That's exactly the case with this. Nothing inside the container can be changed, which greatly improves reliability and testability. so yeah, I'm just giving, just differentiating the containers from your environment of containers on your ship.

1.  Consistency. Like I said, it holds its contents unchanged whether it's on a truck, ship, or train, the container remains there. And like that Docker keeps your app unchanged across your laptop or the cloud.
2.  Portability factor. One standard shipping steal it doesn't need different waiters. One Docker image can run on Windows, Mac, or Linux, the same way the containers can be used on any vehicle.
3.  Talking about efficiency, obviously this increases efficiency when you're talking about development or development practices since all you have to do is create a Docker file for your app and dependencies which we'll look into later, but it's very efficient for development.

Let's talk about their core features one by one.

1.  Secure read-only by design. What you put in your container is immutable! Just repeating the fact that this is an important point, so once built, no process inside can alter it, even the code inside the container. Changing things will produce a new container with a new tag which we'll see later.
2.  Resource control. This is where the comparison between VMs and containers comes into place. Containers are very lightweight. You don't need your complete host OS resources for a container. It lets you strictly limit resources per container when you're talking about CPU quotas, memory limits, or network isolation.

This is a slide comparing VMs and containers, even more. What kind of VM is it on the left? Type 1 or type 2? What kind of VM is this? Two types of VMs here: Type 1 and Type 2? No? Since the hypervisor is on the infrastructure, it's Type 1 because you're directly putting the hypervisor on the kernel and this leads to a more secure VM. But then you have your other type of VM where you've got an operating system between your hypervisor and infrastructure. It's put on the host OS, and then you can have multiple guest OS.

Let's compare VMs and containers with each point. When you talk about virtual machines, each VM has its own guest OS and then dependencies and then your app. What does that mean? That means full OS per instance and a separate kernel.

But in talking about containers, you have a shared OS kernel which doesn't mean they have the same OS, but the kernel is shared, but OS is still independent of each other. Obviously, since you have a separate OS for each instance, you have higher isolation with VMs and you have moderate isolation. I wouldn't say it's completely, really good at isolation, but still, each container runs independent of the other, so that's one good point.

And then, obviously, VMs have a larger footprint because when you have to boot up an instance, you have to set up your memory, you have to set up your storage. It's in gigabytes of data, and if you don't have that space in your host OS, you really can't set up VMs. And containers, we usually talk about in millibytes, so the footprint is pretty small.

And then, to boot up a VM, it's pretty slow to start because you're essentially starting a new system on your system. But when we talk about Docker, all you have to do is run one command, and then your content is set up. Docker is an open-source platform that popularized containers for standardized tooling. First, let's see the terms and the concepts. The journey of cloud computing is there, with so many milestones on this journey. We will understand that in the development of computer technologies, IBM is a very important company. I will say IBM made the biggest contribution previously. Now, we see IBM is not as popular as Apple, Amazon, Google, etc. But IBM has been very important because IBM developed the first virtual machine. Virtual machine is the core or virtualization is the soul of cloud or a major technology of cloud. Without virtualization, we cannot have cloud. And also, IBM proposed timesharing. So, you know the concept of timesharing means that we want to share the resources. You use it this time, I use it another time, not sequentially. We can use the resources, pieces of resources, at the same time. This is the original idea of cloud computing, actually. This was proposed by IBM in 1958. It's a long time ago, so they laid a very solid foundation for cloud computing and Triple W launched in 1991. We have the hardware, which is internet. And then, on top of the hardware, we need software. We have the bookshelf, now we need books. The books are Triple W, it's software-authentic. After we have Triple W, we can share resources on the web. We are the cloud computing boom boom after that.
So, you can see from 1991 to 2002, within these eleven years, is it eleven years? Ten years? How many years is that? Eleven years, okay. Amazon cloud, Amazon was released. At the very beginning, Amazon was not very good, their services were very bad, even internally. It's very hard for them to manage. The reason why it's so good now is they had big problems internally. At that time, to manage the services, they tried to make the APIs for both themselves and their customers to use the APIs. So, that's why it developed so well after they found all those problems. And then, we had Hadoop, which is for big data. And then, is Google GCP? Where is Azure? Azure is here, 2010. Azure is 8 years late than Amazon's cloud services. That's the major reason why AWS has the biggest spectrum of cloud services nowadays. An IT resource is a physical or virtual It could be physical or virtual Now we are not in the context of cloud So, it can't be physical IT related artifacts can be either software-based, such as virtual server or custom software program, or hardware-based, such as physical server or virtual server or natural device. Any devices you can find in the context of IT, it can be used as an IT resource. Resource is a very good word to generally describe the things we are using in the context of IT. Now let me give you some information about the IT resources previously at DeepSense and now on AWS. In 2018, I told you we constructed the data center of DeepSense. This is the picture of DeepSense at that time. We had a few rows of racks in the racks, we have servers, switches, power distribution units, etc. And there are UPS (uninterrupted power supply) in the room. There are AC in the room, and then this data center is constructed. So, when I log in, we can remotely log in to the data center. There is a header or a login node (only one). There's only one login node because we try to reduce the public-facing servers. That is, there's only one server that is public-facing. So, users can log in that node and interact with all other nodes internally using an internal network (not a private IP). So, when you log on the whatever server is in the data center of DeepSense at that point, it's on-premise. As long as you log on one server, you are on that server. You are using the actual physical resources on that server. You are using the memory, you're using the CPU, the hard drive, etc. You are on the machine. That's on-premise.

So, I told you before in the last lecture that we moved to the cloud in 2022, and we are using all three AWS, Air, and GCP. But we focus more on AWS. After we move to AWS, we still remotely use servers. Those servers are still in some data centers, however, we are not using the exact physical resources on flat servers. The resources on those servers are virtualized. You are using a piece of the virtualized machine for your work. So if there is one machine, you can virtualize the machine into several pieces to allow multiple people to use those pieces. And they are software-defined.

We're going to learn more about those virtual machines in the future, so you will understand how we can save the status of the machine. Why it can be removed and started again and again. And you can save money when you stop it. And then when you get down to start it, it can start working. So we save a lot of money!

Located on the premises of the IT enterprise or on-premise, for sure.

The data center or the IT resources staying within the boundary of an organization doesn't necessarily mean it's not a cloud. That doesn't mean it's not a cloud. It depends on how your users are using the resources on the servers or on the resources.

So, an IT resource that is on-premise cannot be cloud-based, and vice-versa. A major difference between the on-premise and the cloud-based IT resources is virtualization. That's the major technology to make them different. In simple terms, this usually means a server running within an organization's own building or data center, connected directly to the internet, serving something up. There are IT resources an organization wishes to eliminate when transitioning into a cloud. Exactly that example I gave you for data center. Every data center has a lifetime of five years, so when we reach the fourth year of the lifetime, we started discussing are we going to go to the cloud or are we going to buy more servers or upgrade the servers? It's very expensive, okay? It could be millions of dollars. So, at that time, a lot of companies when they view their machines are too old, they will think about are we going to move to the cloud or are we going to spend more money to buy the servers? That's the time to make the decision.
Now, let's look at a cloud definition. You don't need to memorize this, okay? It's very long, but I think it covers almost everything. Cloud computing is a model of enabling a convenient, on-demand network access to a shared pool of configurable computing resources like networks, servers, storage, applications, and services. Everything can be shared on the cloud that can be rapidly provisioned and released with a minimal management effort. This is the major benefit of one of the major benefits of cloud maintenance is so minimal or server service provider interaction. This cloud model promotes availability and is composed of five essential characteristics:
1. On-demand self-service
2. Broad network access
3. Resource pooling
4. Quick elasticity
5. Measured service
There are three service models:
1. SAS (Software as a Service)
2. TAS (Platform as a Service)
3. IaaS (Infrastructure as a Service)
There's another one that is FAAs. I believe you will learn more about FAAs in your service course because it's a service for me for deployment models: private, community, public, hybrid.
Whether you learn all these in future lectures, the key enabling technology is:
1. We need a network
2. We need a powerful, inexpensive server and computers
We don't have to use high-end servers to virtualize the resources to offer them to our users and high performance virtualization
This is the key point. Virtualization for commodity hardware now.For the history of the development of cloud computing, what technology you can think of? Let me know. We write it down. You haven't learned cloud yet? Guess. Right? You can guess whatever you think of.

Now let's look at scaling. We said that scaling is very important and scaling is a major benefit of cloud. Scaling means that we need to increase or decrease the resources depending on our use cases or depending on the changing demand from users, right? So scaling from an IT resource perspective represents the ability of IT resources to handle increased or decreased usage demand. Horizontal scaling, here are two major scaling methods. Very simple to understand and they occur in a lot of discussions, blogs, and articles we have to memorize. Understand and memorize because they occur too often. So when you see these terms in the future you should not have any problems. So horizontal scaling is the allocation of or release of IT resources that have the same time, same as the key word over here. Say if I have conversion machine 1 now because the demand is increasing I will have 10 more identical conversion machine 1 that's the same right that's the same IT resource. That's called horizontal scaling. Scaling out or scaling in, scaling out means I'm going to increase the same identical, the same kind of IT resources so getting in in the other way right we are going to decrease the number of the same kind of IT resources. Very easy to understand right? Vertical scaling is the replacement of an existing IT resource by another with higher or lower capacity say now I have virtual machine one my memory is uh 50 gigabytes I found out it's not enough for my application to run I need to increase it when I increase it it's a vertical scale then you are replacing the current memory from a 50 gig one to 100 gig one or 150 gig one right that's virtual scaling you can also decrease the value the number of the amount of a memory or cpu I had 10 cpu cores and now I need 20 cpu cores that's also vertical scaling so you are going to change the IT resource you are not going to get the copies of the same IT resource okay so they are called scaling up or scaling down if you uh make the replacing the with higher capacity bigger capacity is scaling up lower scaling down okay this two these two concepts are very important now let's understand their major horizontal scaling is less expensive because it's the same IT resource you don't need to add more resources right so vertical scaling could be more expensive it doesn't mean it has to be more expensive it could be if you are downsizing your your memory or cpu you are saving money right so it could be more expensive IT resources instantly available so if you want to you provision more IT resource identical IT resources is really you don't have a down time of your system it's very quick however for vertical scaling so if your you have a uh your database is running and your database using 50 gig of memory but you're not enough you are going to increase it to a hundred gigabytes in most cases you have to shut down the instance and then increase the memory and then bring it up so yearly vertical scaling takes longer than horizontal skating and in your real projects horizontal If you have memory of 50 megabytes you don't need any more resources. You replace the current one with bigger memory. For horizontal scaling it's not limited by hardware capacity. You can keep adding new machines right but for horizontal scaling for any machine there is a limit of the number of CPU cores and the number of the amount of memories right so there is a limit for vertical scaling that's the major difference. Okay, we still have risks and challenges using cloud. Definitely, we put our data in another person's hands. What are the problems? Multi-tenancy is definitely the cause of the problem, just like when you share apartments with other people. Even if you yourself have a one-bedroom apartment, you don't need to worry about any other people who steal your stuff, who use your stuff, who abuse your stuff, right? But if you share, there is a certain degree of uncertainty. There could be some security issues theoretically. There could be. Because technically, that can happen.

So multi-tenancy is also the root cause of the security issue or concerns from cloud consumers. Because you have some overlapping traffic problems. But cloud is very safe, as long as you are professional, as long as you know how to manage IAM and apply the cybersecurity matters. Almost all the problems are caused by humans, not by the tools. A lot of humans are not professional on their security job, that's why problems occur.

So your data leaving your premises means increasing risk. No matter what you do, your responsibility for protecting the data is now shared with your cloud provider. It's in their systems, right? You do feel not safe, so you have relinquished control. Do you trust your provider? Can you trust AWS? I think we can trust AWS. Can you trust Donatron? AWS is a company that belongs to the U.S. So Donatron can do anything to the company. He can do anything nowadays. No one can control them, so he may shut down the services to Canada if he forces AWS to shut down the services to Canada. We lose our services, we lose our data, we lose our business. So you never know who you can trust. A year ago, we didn't know Donatron is like this, right? No one can imagine a person, a president, can behave like this, like a crazy person, right?

So limited portability. There is a use case that's very interesting. It's Porter's use case. It's about three or four years ago. This company is a social media company. They fully run on AWS, everything is on AWS. However, they violated the policies, the rules of AWS. AWS told Potter they told Potter to move their products off AWS within one week. It's a big company, it's not small. How can they move their products off AWS within one week? They tried to move GCP and Azure, but got rejected. They don't trust them, those two cloud providers don't trust Potter. They had a very huge problem because their business could be gone. They are fully locked-in on AWS, so this is a huge issue.

Limited portability. If your strategy at the very beginning is multi-cloud, your applications are running on multi-cloud, you are much safer, right? Even if one cloud removes you from their platform, you can still have other clouds. But you have to follow all the rules and policies. Like for Potter, all the three major cloud providers rejected them to reside on their cloud platform. And multi-regional compliance and the legal issues. So if you have data on the cloud, your lovely angels will look at your data and talk about data gossip. Your data is in another person's hand, especially in a different country, different region. So when there are problems, who should you play? Is your data violating the policies or the laws in that country? When that problem happens, do you follow the law of that country or your current country? Is AWS responsible for that, or you are fully responsible for that? So there will be a lot of problems when those kind of issues arise. And there are also multiple hand problem. When breaches happen, who is responsible? Who to blame? There are SLAs, service level agreement, right? There are SLAs, but still, is the SLA precise enough to protect you, right? Cloud providers will try to point a finger to you, it's your responsibility, okay? Actually, in most cases, it is your responsibility. They are very professional. They know their stuff, we may not know that well about their stuff. So we make mistakes. So in a lot of cases, we lose the win.
Also, for environment is very important nowadays. Every company, when they propose to do some project nowadays, they have to mention if they are going to contribute to the climate control. For the approval of the project. Using cloud services you are contributing to the climate control. Why? Because they try to use green energy, carbon-free. They try to use the solar panels, like GCP Google use a full mountain of solar panels to power their data centers. How can they do that? At Dalhousie, we waste a lot of money on power because we are not using green power, right? We just use the power and we waste a lot of power. So moving to the cloud can save a lot and make a big contribution to the climate control. Although data centers of cloud make big carbon emissions nowadays, but they are on their way. See, they are on their way to the carbon-free energy and high work. Eventually, it's going to be beneficial to the environment.

  Every cloud provider has to rely on ISP (Internet Service Provider), where you guys are using like a power line and Rogers etcetera are ISPs. ISPs are connected with the backbone of the Internet.
  The backbones of the Internet most of them are in the United States, so that's why people would like to use US East one because US East one is very close to the backbone of internet infrastructure.
  Do you think for the Internet, our internet is connected from continent to continent? Do you think there are cables in the water? And in the sea? Are we connected with that? Yes, we are connected with that, but do you think cloud providers also have that kind of cable and in the sea?
  Cloud providers have the same thing. Cloud providers also have cables under the sea. You cannot imagine how powerful these cloud providers nowadays. They have as complex infrastructure as the Internet and they better network topology and protocols for their within their own network.
  In the future, when we learn network, I'm gonna tell you the differences between them and the performance is way better than a normal internet.We stay safe. This is our house or our apartment, right? So we will have our firewall and our modem or switches, and we need to go outside of our switch, right? Our modem to be connected with ISP network. And ISP's network is connected with the backbone home ISP network. Claude provider's network is connected with the ISP's network. ISP's network is connected with the backbone internet infrastructure. So that's how we get into the Claude provider's network. There are a few hops, right? This is the way how we get the ubiquitous access from all over the world.   Now let's look at the bandwidth. When we look at bandwidth, we have two concepts to understand:

1. The bandwidth itself
2. The throughput
   When you subscribe to cloud provider services, they will give you the number of the bandwidth. The number of the bandwidth is not an actual traffic that is for your service; it's a theoretical bandwidth for the network. Really, how much data we can transfer on their network is lower than the bandwidth. It's like there is a maximum rate at which data can be transferred for a network, it's the theoretical upper limit of data transfer capacity.
   So, whenever you hear the bandwidth of a network, it means that's the maximum, theoretically. The actual data transfer should be lower or even much lower than that bandwidth. The unit could be, I believe, we have BPS nowadays, even KBPS. At least, we are using M or G in BPS. The analogy is like the width of a highway. If our highway is eight lanes, theoretically, we should not have any problem allowing five cars as an eight cars to run at the same time on the highway and reach their destination without a problem. If you imagine if there is one thing, if there's one car that has a problem on one lane, that lane is blocked, right? That's the major problem in Halifax. Halifax's streets are all very narrow. A lot of streets we only have two lanes. After one lane is blocked, that the big traffic jam will occur, right? That happens so many times in Halifax. Halifax's traffic is among the worst in North America. Is among the worst. Throughput is the actual data transfer rate, so the actual rate at which data is successfully transferred. When we say lanes of a highway, if a car cannot reach the destination successfully, we don't say that lane works, right? The data is successfully transferred over a network path in a given period, so we reflect the real-world performance of the network. So analogy is like the number of cars that actually make it to the destination on the highway, but there could be a lot of problems like accidents on the highway. On the network, we have natural congestion and latency, packet loss, and other inefficiencies, so our throughput is always lower than bandwidth.

Latency is a latency frequently mentioned by professors or someone in any courses or project. It's a latency very frequently mentioned, maybe in a network course. In your projects, is this frequently mentioned? Or so? Okay, nice.

So latency I'm going to mention latency again and again in the future because latency is almost one of the most important performance metrics when we measure the value of the performance of your application. So when you have a source point and you go to your destination (say this is your data or your packets, this is your destination), your data is going to travel from the original place to the destination. If there are multiple stops, the latency will add, right? In the future when you do real-time, real industry projects, this happens often.

Let me give you an example. I used to work with a company Halifax. They try to detect the leak of the hydrogen in a tank. They have the valve involved installed at the top of that tank and they have some sensors installed beside the valve. So the data has to be sent to somewhere to process it. If there is a threshold, if it's above some threshold, it is a leak, right? Otherwise, it's safe.

So the data is collected, the data is not big. But the bottom line of that issue was that it's 24/7, it's every single second. First, we need to send it to a storage to store it (say if we send it to a laptop, or we send it to a cloud storage, or we send it to our on-site edge device, like they use called Raspberry). We can send it to Raspberry Pi, right? Multiple solutions, okay? We'll give different processing times, right? If you send it to the cloud, it takes some time. If you send it to the local laptop, it could be connected with a cable. If you send it to the on-site Raspberry Pi, it's even shorter. It's on-site, right? So this is part of the calculation of the latency.

Then you need to process the data. When you process the data, you need some compute power, you need a CPU and the memory. That's the basic thing for processing. So, cloud, your laptop, and your Raspberry Pi they have different processing times of your data.

The company operates with six dedicated layers of security.

1. Signage and fencing - this is the first piece of security that is easily and visibly noticed.
2. Secured perimeter with thermal cameras and overlapping fields of view - a person can be tracked.
3. Building accessibility - in order to enter the building, access has to be authorized. RFID badges, security personnel, and biometric scanning are all used in conjunction with one another as well as check points located throughout the building.
4. Security operations center - this is where the cameras are monitored. These work hand-in-hand with security personnel, both on the floor and behind the screens.
5. Data for security is set up to only have specific people authorized to have access. This floor's access through the integration of the customer having an added security bonus of encrypting their data. This means that even though the engineer or technician may have access to the floor, their data is encrypted with a special key that makes it to where the technician can not access the customer's information. Number six, last but not least, we have the dedicated hardware destruction protocols and procedures that are in place to offer the most effective form of security. When a hard drive is ready for destruction, it is then passed to the destruction room via a two-way locker. This makes it to where only authorized personnel that are located within the drive shredding room will have access to the drives. Adding to this, the metal detectors are in place to ensure that no property is ever removed. Now let's look at availability and reliability. Availability we understand we need to have redundancies, right? That's the availability. We measure how many times our systems got failed or how many failures we had in the past year. So, they can work with each other to measure the availability. Availability is measured in terms of time loss or time up. Actually, reliability is measured in terms of the number and impact of failures. You want to expect your app and service is up and running! That's the principle for whatever security setup, physically or for our software.

CPUs, we understand CPUs very well, right? We've been using it for a long time, and memory in our common machines we use just common memories, but in data center they use ECC (Error Correction C). What does the C mean? Error Correction, I forgot what the last C means.

Okay, so this kind of RAM has an extra bit added to every single byte. You know, in every single byte of your data, there are 0s and 1s, right? So, there are either an even number or an odd number of 1s in your byte. So, this extra bit stores the number of 1s in your byte. So, every single byte has an extra bit to store this information. So, when we detect this bit, if the value of this bit is changed, that means your value was changed, right? If your value should not be changed, the byte should not be changed, that means there's an error, and there is a very advanced algorithm on the chip that can, in most cases, correct the error by itself.

So, when pretty long time ago when I was managing the data center, when I read the log files, I could see there was a correctable error on some memory names, okay, so that means it's fine. There was an error, but it's correctable by the chips themselves by the algorithm. If it's not correctable, you will have to replace it. Get it? So, you cannot buy ECC memories anywhere, okay, because, in most cases, we buy them just as normal ECC memories. You need to look at the features of your memory and look at exactly the same memory.

Storage is like you saw those big arrays of storage, right? So, they are physically there, very many hard drives, but they are logically organized in different ways. So, when you have arrays of hard drives, you can logically say, "This part is a hundred terabytes, I can format it, and I can logically make this part as our home directory." Then, I allocate every single user one terabyte of home directory. So, when the user accesses their home directory, their data is actually in these drives physically, but they never know that. They only see a home directory, got it? So, this is the RAID array, okay, RAID array. RAID has different levels, from level 0 to level 10, different RAID arrays have different abilities to store redundant data. For some RAIDs, we have one copy of the original data, so when there is a single drive that fails, you will get an alert, no problem, because most systems can tolerate two failed drives. So, when you see one failed drive, you will order that drive right away and then replace it, okay. You won't wait for two drives to fail, and it's very rare for the drives to fail very frequently in a data set because those are all very good quality hard drives. We can also map this part, say, this part we have 200 terabytes, we can logically make a directory, so everyone has 5 terabytes. Data is in these hard drives. Just at a high level we need to understand the arrays of the drives. So when we have the after we logically allocate the drives to different amount of directories when we want to our servers to access them we need to put the drives or the storage in the network. So really we connect our storage with fiber cables to access the data in a very fast way. And also in the network attached storage there is a big controller. The controller has a big cache here. So when you read some data, the data will be stored in the cache in the controller. Next time when you read the same data it's going to be from the cache so it's very fast. So for any storage in the future like the database is also kind of storage right? Database and S3, object storage, whatever you are using in the future as long as it's about storage there must be a solution of caching. Okay? There must be a solution of caching because we always want to use caching for improving performance especially for distributed databases. You can just put some bullet points for your answer, Zhang, for each slide, and then we can start.The design should be able to read fast and read simple documents, not very complex ones. By doing relational linking, we would increase the complexity of the study tracker, which would end up increasing the cost of it, which is against what we aimed for when we started the project. We wanted it to be something free and usable for every student.
On the next slide, this is for the third question, third part of question seven: what happens when the data grows? It's pretty simple. With the accumulation of many tasks, we will eventually lead to a Firestore performance degradation. A linking task to session would create many relationships, which are expensive cross-collection queries, and this would eventually reach a size limit for the document.
Next slide: what would be the solution to change this? We must first of all keep or adopt a solution that is NoSQL friendly. We have three paths that we are going to see:
1. The lightweight reference: simply adding task IDs to each task when it is created. This would store them and link them to the session they are in. We would have fast lookups to the task whenever we look for one. The document growth would be very minimal and it would be adequate and suitable for work or small and medium data sets. Once it starts getting bigger, we would run into trouble.
2. The reverse lookup by adding session IDs, so each session would have its own ID and that ID would be contained in the task. This would help manage a bigger set of tasks, but it is less scalable overall.
3. The linking collection, where each link would be its own document. We would have an unlimited number of tasks and sessions. We would avoid the large arrays issue, which would clearly be a big one if we end up going that way. The good thing about it is that the student could use it to link it with other study platforms like Google Classroom or Trello, etc.
These are the three main paths that would be helpful. The probably the most suitable one for a project of this size would be the lightweight, which is task IDs.

Today's lecture is a guest lecture on contracts, consideration for your work, and copyright, and we're joined by Diana Burns, who is a lawyer here in Halifax doing work specifically with the community through the Legal Information Society of Nova Scotia.
Hello all you virtual peeps and all the people here in attendance. It is lovely to be speaking with you today. I was asked by Professor Mascara to come in and talk about contracts and copyright, so thank you for the invite. I always like coming in to chat about this stuff, especially since this particular area is in a lot of flux. There's a lot that's happening right now in the law and in culture and in society, and computer scientists are right at the heart of the majority of these considerations, ethical debates, financial disputes, copyright disputes, all kinds of stuff we'll talk about today. I understand if there's any questions; Professor Mascara will just say, "Hey, there's a question about this." I think, because it's in person and virtual, we can interrupt at any point and just clarify in the moment for people who have questions. Otherwise, we will also have a little bit of time at the end, so we'll see how that all goes.
Yes, as was mentioned, I'm a lawyer. I'm here today as a part of the Legal Information Society of Nova Scotia, which is a nonprofit in Nova Scotia that delivers education and information about the laws and kind of highlights where Nova Scotia is a little different from other places, where Canada is a bit different from other places, and what applies here versus some other jurisdictions. Shout out to this one.
Contents: the aim is to talk about these three things:
- Contracts: why they matter
- Employment services versus for-hire agreements (employee versus independent contractor)
- Intellectual property and your work, focusing specifically on copyright because of the aspects related to what we're talking about that are more specific to copyrighted material like source code
We'll talk a little bit about that, how that is treated by the laws, and review.
I like to start with contract, because underneath pretty much everything related to what we're talking about today is a really ancient concept of contract. We have contract in every part of the world, in every culture. This idea of doing something or having something done and paying for it in some way and expecting the people to do the things or to provide the things, or whatever it may be, is that one, two, three step in all the common law countries, the Commonwealth, particularly those countries. We inherited a lot of that sort of Roman influence law, and so you'll see things that are very, very familiar, with different little tweaks in every different jurisdiction.
When we talk about the common law of contract, we're talking about all those Commonwealth countries out there, or formerly Commonwealth countries specifically, but also civil jurisdictions influenced by France, civil codes, which are also still receiving some kind of version of this contract law. What we're going to narrow in on is, because Canada is a Commonwealth jurisdiction mainlybasically, it means that there's a whole body of decisions that judges make that actually sort of find law. Some people think it means the judges are making law, but the whole system is kind of built on the idea that the judges are finding the law through the facts related to what legislation is in place or what other decisions other judges have made in the past. It's looking at a whole history, so you'll still see cases from the 1800s referenced today, 1700, 1600, because the rule was made at that point and it carries all the way through.
When we're talking about that, the idea of contract is embedded in all of those historical ideas. What we've done over the years is make a whole bunch of different pieces of legislation, different acts, different laws, codes related to contracts, so that's a sample:
- Labour Standards Code
- Electronic Commerce Act
- Sale of Goods Act
- Consumer Protection Act
- Statute of Frauds
- Many, many, many more that are all related to contracts in Nova Scotia
That kind of tweaks that old common law contract idea. That idea is that one, two, three: you have an offer, you have acceptance, and then you have consideration. When we're thinking about contracts, we're actually just talking about agreements. Agreements that are enforceable in some way, so an agreement that you can get help from someone else to actually have some result, to get some relief or a remedy. An offer is really hard to see on this one.
The offer is the starting point. Everyone who's listening or here has offered something and been involved in a contract. That could be an offer to sell something on people still use Marketplace, an offer to sell on one of these platforms. It can be any sort of starting point where you have something you want to offer to the public or to a specific group of the public. You put it out there. Here's the offer. Verbal or writing doesn't matter. As far as the legality of it, it's just that you have an offer and you put it out to the world.
It could be an offer for a car, to sell your car, your used car. You list it, you describe it, right? You put it out there; you're offering it to the world. You're going to accept offers.
The second part of a contract is reception. Your car is out there; you've described it. Now it's time to see who will accept your offer. Acceptance is required. They talk about needing of the minds. You have to have the understanding of what the thing is, right? It has to be this silver car, not that black car. It's a specific thing, and then that's what's being accepted, so you can't accept something if it is not the thing, for example, that you were offered, right? Someone offers you the silver car; you say, "Yeah, I'll take that black car." There is no contract there, right? People may be talking about buying cars, but you have to have acceptance. It has to be clear. It has to be specifically related to the offer.
We also have counteroffers, which start a new offer. Sometimes, when people are specifically talking about a job offer and they say, "This is the job I was offered, but I have some concerns and I don't want that offer, but I'm willing to take everything except the salary." That's a counteroffer. It's actually considered a new offer, so it's a new offer of old terms plus this or minus this that creates a new offer which may or may not be accepted. As soon as you counteroffer, the law says you've actually refused the first offer.
As we're going through talking about employment and independent contracting and stuff like that, just keep in mind that when you do go back with sort of an update or a change to an initial offer, it is like you're rejecting and then starting with a different offer where you're now the offeree. "I will take all of your terms plus this or minus that." The third thing is consideration. In order for a contract to be enforceable, it has to have something that is exchanged for it, a value exchange. Consideration can be found in money; it can be found in things or other services. So there's been an offer for the silver car. The silver car is what the person wants; they accept it clearly. They have to pay the $3,500 a month for that silver car in order to actually close the deal, in order to actually facilitate the payments. You are then giving the consideration. Facilitating that through financing agreements or other things like that fulfills that conditional requirement.

Conditions can be many things, right? It can be multiple things; it can be over time. The idea is there has to be an agreed amount, right? The agreed amount is $3,500 for 12 months, paid monthly. Now we have consideration. If I don't pay all that and I haven't met all the consideration, the contract still exists, but now the person who actually did the offer can take things back, right? Because you haven't fulfilled all of the requirements.

Nice clean contracts obviously have consideration all done in one go, but there's lots of contracts like employment contracts where you're doing things over a longer period of time and you're getting paid over a longer period of time. That initial contract is still what you're working under, right? Promises do not count as contracts, so what does that mean?

There's a classic case where, actually in the US, a girl sued her dad because he promised her a luxury vehicle when she graduated with a certain letter degree for her high school certificate or whatever they call it, diplomas. She basically went to court because the relationship had fallen apart. She said, "I have a contract, an agreement with him, and he wrote it in a card. He actually wrote it to her in a birthday card, kind of thing, a congratulations card, was not a birthday card, and said, 'If you get this in advance, I will get you this luxury vehicle.'"

She didn't get the luxury vehicle; he changed his mind, and she sued. The court said, in keeping with all this, one, two, three, offer, acceptance, consideration. There was an offer, there was acceptance, but there was no consideration. There was no value that she was putting into it that actually resulted in that being enforceable. Even though she performed what he had agreed would be a condition, the court said no, because he promised it to her, because it was gratuitous, because it was just a gift. If certain things happened, the court did not actually uphold it. The difference between those two cases is that you absolutely can force the benefit of a bonus if you achieve a certain target that they've set, and they have attached all of the key pieces. The offer is you get this many sales; you've accepted that, so you reach and actually get the sales, and the consideration is an lump sum amount of money. In employment law, that's going to be sufficient to equal a contract.

The judge in that case, and this is a bit of a debated case, said this was a promise of the father to do something, and it's not enforceable because she had no intention of any consideration to actually expect her grade achievement. Her grade achievement would result in an enforceable claim. There's quite a bit of a tension in that, because she did what she was asked to do and he said he would do it in writing, and he failed. The key piece that they kind of focus on is when you're talking about consideration, it has to be specifically outlined to the degree that it can be enforceable through the courts. Promising some great gift, if someone does something, is a conditional thing; it's not consideration. Whereas a dollar figure related to employment work and achieving a specific target is considered to have perfected that piece. It's considered to have solidified that consideration, and you can enforce those and do enforce those.

There are a lot of people who think he should have had to buy the car. She didn't appeal it, but yeah, so yes, the idea is the contract's legally binding, so even things that look like contracts that seem to fit in a fact scenario, the thing at the end of the day is, can I enforce it through the courts? Will the judges think that it was just a promise, or they really did agree that this is exactly what was supposed to be done? Was there really acceptance? That is the key difference between that promise and actually being able to use the courts to get the remedy. This is sometimes really difficult to identify, the difference between what one of those three aspects actually entails and what actually happens on the facts.

Last comment about that car case: I think, at the end of the day, it was just because they were family. I think, based on the way the judge wrote it, that family difference made it seem like the consideration wasn't within the scope of her actually expecting performance. It was kind of like a dream.

Why we like written agreements: regardless of the fact that it is completely legal to have a verbal agreement, a verbal agreement in Nova Scotia is enforceable if I offer, you accept it, you pay the price, and I have to transfer the thing to you, whether it's in writing or not. Having a written agreement is really good for protecting you if you're looking to enforce the agreement because the other side has refused in some way, in order to have the courts help you. Number one, you've got some proof, evidence to actually show the court, so it's not just he said, she said, they said, they said. It is actually something you can point to that was documented or sat down in a way that a judge can read it and get their own impression.

Number two, you can show what specific clause you're saying was a breach. You might have acceptance of an offer, and it's really clear in the contract that acceptance was you show up and pick up the thing at 7:15 p.m. at this location. When it comes to the actual consideration part of it, if they didn't pay the money through this particular way, then okay, we now have proof that it was broken, it was breached. We call it the contract term of consideration. For example, they didn't actually pay the money, so everything else that's in writing is there. They're saying, "Oh, I wasn't supposed" So having that proof is really invaluable, even though a verbal employment agreement is still enforceable. I did a talk like this previously, and someone asked about the internship question because co-ops and internships have really contradictory information available.

The key thing I wanted to point out for people who are engaged in that process is that there is an exception here in Canada and in Nova Scotia, which is really sort of suspicious in a lot of ways. Basically, if the internship is a part of a formal education program and you're getting a credit for the work, it can be unpaid work. When we're talking about employment contracts, you've got an offer to do an internship, and it turns out it's an unpaid internship. It has to qualify through some formal education component within your school.

The idea is that an intern actually shouldn't be doing the work of an employee, either in federally regulated workplaces or provincial. In reality, they usually are, but what's supposed to happen is they're supposed to be shadowing or supposed to not be doing the work that an employee would be doing. They're supposed to be learning, following, gaining the experience. It's unpaid, and then they get an educational credit for completing that.

It's just something to keep in mind. There's been talk about having at least minimum wage paid to everybody. Some internships are paid. Lots of internships are paid, but there's still a whole bunch of them that remain unpaid, and that's why, because they use that exception that there's an educational component and they're not acting as employees, which is arguable. There have been some people who sued successfully for doing internships with an expectation that they would be paid, because some educational credit didn't come to them for some reason. They did all this work and they didn't get the educational credit, not even because of what they did. They successfully completed it.

There are still some grey areas, so we'll see in time if that changes, but at the moment Canada's still one of these places where, surprisingly, you can do work and not be paid for it except by way of a credit or a school requirement.

Shifting now to focus on employees and independent contractors, shifting to the world of employment. Why this is important for a lot of you who are already engaged in work or going out to accept contracts and going out on job sites. The idea is to kind of talk about the differences, because there are some, particularly when we get to talking about copyright and intellectual property.

The basis for working in Canada and Nova Scotia is that you can work for an entity, for a business, for a person as an employee, or you can carry on business yourself and be an independent contractor. You might be doing the exact same work in both circumstances, but an employee is treated very differently than an independent contractor.

Being an employee, the vast majority of people are employees, right, that are in the workforce. The idea is that there's an employer who has a job. They have specific needs. They create a job to address or provide services for those specific needs, and they hire the person, right? It could be in writing. It could be verbally, and if you are looking at an employment agreement, there should be some basics. They're listed up there.

It should say what your rate of pay is going to be. It should say what your vacation time is. It should say how many holidays you get, like how that works, or point you to a work policy or something. That's the idea with the written contract. There are some basics you should have. Nova Scotia employees can go to the Labor Standards Board if they have complaints against what their boss is doing related to their work. Maybe their boss is getting them to the amount it's required to be if you have to work on a holiday. The Labor Standards Board accepts complaints. They review certain things related to hours and making sure you're getting your wages and that sort of thing. It covers all employees except those that work in unions. If you're a unionized employee, you are dealing through a collective bargaining agreement, which there's a lot of them here on this campus, actually. That collective bargaining agreement takes you out of the Labor Standards Board. If there's problems with the employer and you're a unionized employee, that's why you have union reps. That's why you have a grievance process. That's why there's other things that you go through in order to get relief, satisfaction if there's a problem in a unionized workplace.

The job is not paying any out-of-pocket expenses for the work you're doing. You will be able to get unemployment insurance if your job is ended, you're laid off, something like that. You basically have some built-in protection. If you work for an employer for 10 plus years in Nova Scotia, they cannot fire you without paying you a significant amount of money if they're even allowed to fire you at all. Everyone under 10 years, though, it's a bit of the Wild West. Tenured employees have extra special protection, but if you're not working there for 10 plus years, you are really reading that contract closely and you don't have as many protections.

An independent contractor, on the other hand, is considered to control their business. Even if you are, say, running a small business, it's just yourself. Maybe you're a sole proprietor. Maybe you do some kind of service, maybe for a CEO. You scan in and upload seniors' photos to a cloud, and you set it up for them so that they can design things and put their grandkids' faces on things and all that kind of stuff, but they don't know how to get this stuff onto the internet. That's your job. You're a sole proprietor, and you scan in photos. It's called Scan Me, and you upload it, and these people pay you $29.99 for every 1,000 photos. I don't know. Whatever. That business is a sole proprietorship. You are an independent contractor. You control the hours you work. You control what type of computer you use, what type of scanner you use, what resolution you put it on. You give advice about what platforms your clients should use. You have full control of your business.

Your business might be 10 clients, but it's your business. You're an independent contractor, and then it turns out a larger business says, "Hey, I really like what you're doing. We want to add that to what we do for seniors at this long-term care home. We want to offer what you're doing to the people who live here regularly." Okay, you're not just going to become an employee; you're going to be an independent contractor. You're going to negotiate with that home, with that company who runs that 800-person building over on Quimple, and you're going to say, "Okay, but here's the hours I'll be available, here's the paperwork people have to sign. I have to be paid up front. This is how much a consultation. You give the instructions, and the idea is that you're responsible for your business. You might collect HST. You might deduct the HST afterwards off your invoices yourself. You might handle your business how you see fit, even if you've been contracted to do it for a bigger company or for someone else."

An independent contractor is considered to be equal, even if you're an independent contractor with IBM. If you are your own business, you're providing them some sort of service, but you control a whole bunch of things about what you're doing. The level of control can vary, so some big companies say, "Well, you have to do this, this, this, and this, right?" They want people to have certain materials and certain things and all this kind of stuff. If they want you to do it that way, you ask for more money. "Well, we need more money if we're going to have this kind of technology or this kind of hardware," so it's a back and forth, right? The law considers you equal, even if one is a billion-dollar company and one is a $25,000 company. The law looks at and protects each if they come to the court asking for help.

When you're an independent contractor, you could incorporate a business. You could just be working yourself, sometimes. Independent contractors, especially in technology fields or freelancers of some kind, and the labor standards board does not actually do any support if you're an independent contractor. Just like if you're a unionized employee, you're separate, taken out of the labor standards process. You are your own business; you can roll it, and you have the full power to negotiate and ask questions and do whatever it might be.

Some things to kind of consider when it comes to am I an employee or an independent contractor, because some people, I was thinking I was an independent contractor, but we didn't have a written contract, and I did have to use their equipment, and I actually went to their workplace to do the work. Maybe I actually was an employee. The idea here is to kind of understand, okay, what is actually the situation I'm in and where can I get benefits from? Even if you were thinking you're an independent contractor, sometimes the government says, "Actually, you were an employee." Actually, because you had to be the person doing the work. You couldn't hire someone else to do the work for you. That's a pretty strong indicator the person's actually an employee.

We don't think it's maybe one or the other; that being told us, so we're gonna do this test. There are some things to consider about what they're calling you, because it has a big impact on your intellectual property. It'll show up in clauses that you're asked to sign an agreement. Employment versus independent contractor in relation to intellectual property has some really big differences.

Intellectual property is kind of in the news now more than ever before, talked about on social media and on late-night shows and all kinds of stuff. Everyone is sort of talking about it right now, right? That's our social intelligence or whatever, sort of news stories coming out about the future of tech and all this kind of stuff. At the end of the day, intellectual property has been around for eons, and it's basically the idea that if you create something, it's yours to sell or to license or to use or to refuse to be let used.

What we did many, many years ago was pass a whole bunch of laws again, mainly in common law countries to start; then it rolled out around the rest of the world. All kinds of positives and negatives with it over the years, but the idea is basically that intellectual property, those trademarks, patents, integrated circuit topologies, industrial designs, copyrights, is all related to the work of a human, is all related to the work of a person being creative. Because of that, it should be specially protected, so it has to be specifically made in a way and then specially protected in another way. The Canadian Intellectual Property Office handles all IP applications, and it has a whole bunch of resources on its website. If you're a bit of an inventor or if you're a creator, the Canadian Intellectual Property Office has a lot of free resources, has a lot of information on ways that you can protect yourself. You look to see what else has been done. Maybe you have an idea; you check to see if it's been done before. Maybe a version of it has, but not totally your version, so you can alter and change that and register that through licensing and a whole bunch of other things.

Copyright is one type of intellectual property, right? The trademarks, the big M logo for McDonald's, that's a trademark. There are some other trademarks, the Apple, so that is a way of showing any kind of R, different kinds of the circled R. They're all ways of showing that something's been registered, but actually that's not a trademark itself, because there are different versions of it. Like, the way that looks, that's a trademark. The idea is it's an emblem or it's a logo or it's something that encapsulates an idea; that's the idea of a trademark.

Copyright is so many things. Copyright is really any creative work that is designed, imagined, documented by a person, by a human. It's the most common form of business asset. Writing instruction manuals, diatom software, computer programs are all copywritable, are all copyright from the moment you make them. In Canada, still copyright is automatic as soon as you create it; it's yours. The reason we register copyright is to protect it from other people, so it's not just stolen, which is something that happens all the time. Like one effect where someone makes a design of a little stuffy animal, and then all of a sudden there's 55 knockoffs available through Team North America and China, fighting over it terribly for the last 40 years and still don't have an agreement. We have really big copyright lawsuits, like Samson Sung in Apple, that resolved themselves after 20 years. Now they just all work together. At the end of the day, when they steal copyrights, it's about money, so they came up with a figure, they came up with a financial agreement. It doesn't mean that they didn't violate copyright, but they came up with an agreement eventually, because I found that they've really had so. Copyright is about that original human creation that should be protected, and as soon as you make it, the law says you own your copyright straight off.

This green in this room is really okay, so it must be original. It must be made by a human. It has to be in some expressed form, so it could be a song, could be an audio of your song. The only thing about a non-disclosure is it's related to more third parties, so it'll say, "Usually, you're not allowed to disclose this to anyone but a lawyer, your personal counsel." Whereas a confidentiality agreement will usually say, "You can't access this in certain places, right? You have to maintain the confidentiality."

So usually, if there's a confidentiality specifically called that, in those areas, like there's certain provinces (like Ontario) that will use that more often. That usually means that not only will you not share the info, but you swear that your system is protected, so it can't be hacked, it can't be accessed by anyone else, right? So there's no one else who has access to your laptop; your laptop is especially password-protected; no one else has access to it. So that confidentiality is like the next level. They're basically the same thing, but it's usually if it's specifically called that, it's like a next level type of non-disclosure. It's not only can't you talk about it or share or use that information, but you have to also protect it yourself when it's in your hands.

Yeah, I mean, so usually they'll have a time frame, but sometimes they will be really, really long time frames. The only thing that is pretty much never upheld is if it's an indefinite time frame. So, it specifically says, "You will never disclose this information. You will never share any confidential information." Usually, it's for a period of time. "You will not share this information for five years." Transferring the copyright. You'll see that sometimes in independent contractor agreements (like we talked about), they may include something that specifically says you agree to transfer copyright to the company by signing this agreement. The specific process of transfer that's usually something you'll see in an independent contractor agreement because the company knows who we're hiring this person to do it, but we still want all of it. We need all of it. For our other employees to use it in the future, for example. We need to make sure that they're also transferring copyright to us, and you'll see that included in those sometimes. Again, waiver of moral rights. You have to specifically sign off on that. You never used to see that. You'll see it more often now, and that's something to really push back against. That basically means that you cannot reliably tell the world that you did the thing. That's never good for anyone.

Right? So giving away your moral rights unless they're paying you a lot of money, you shouldn't do it as default. You shouldn't because they can block you from being allowed to speak about it, they can block you from being able to use it as a reference piece in the future. It's really restrictive. 100%! 100%! 100%! That's why it was so rare, but you'll see it a little bit more now where people are trying to pretend it's theirs. They're trying to pretend that they came up with it, and if that person wavers that moral right if they sign it away specifically and they get some sort of consideration for it, even if that consideration is minimal, it's going to be almost impossible to reverse. And yeah, it has a really big impact because even if you're not making money off of the thing in the future, you want, by right, to be able to say, "I did that!" That example we were looking at earlier is a great way of saying it. That Victoria person will always be able to say, "I did that!" Yes, it's owned by these people. Maybe I sold it to them. Maybe I worked for them. Maybe I was an independent contractor for them. Maybe I worked for them as an employee. They still recorded properly that I designed it.

A provision requiring the employee to assist the employer in obtaining protection. You will see this in more like well-paid positions where you're doing things that you're creating things in the job environment. There's a high likelihood that there will be an end product that's going to make money. So that's when you'll start seeing those all the time. But you have to even if you go work somewhere else 5 years later, they call you up under this clause. You're required to sign off to help them get something or come back and testify in a court case, whatever it might be. So it's kind of a hang-on provision.

To kind of cap off what we've been talking about, make sure that the agreements you enter into really refer and reflect and you really understand stuff that's important to you.
