In the last class, we talked about React Flux architecture and we talked about the general category of the components that it has being container components or presentation components. In this class, I go a little bit more by talking about the specific components. In terms of React Flux architecture, or if we want to think about how React sees a page, it essentially sees a page as different, different modules. The modules can be logical modules; they can be presentation modules; they can be sections of your page.
For example, here with the example I just showed you, we have:
- a search bar
- our navigation bar
- a user controller bar
- a category container
Let me go back. You see, that's a category container, and then we have an ad container at the bottom, which you can see here and the bottom section. Not including the search bar, the navigation bar, or the user controller bar, basically becomes your main container of the content you're going to have. Every application, when you're using React, for the most part I could say I would extrapolate that a lot of other frameworks would see sections on a page in a similar way, not necessarily always exactly the same, but they all kind of go along the same way. Some of them, the syntax is different; some of them, how we link them is different; some of them, how we pass data or how we update state is different. They all have their differences, but in general they all have different components for sections of a page.
Every React application is made up of smaller pieces of code, as I mentioned. We call them components. Every component in React is derived from either a functional component or a class component, regardless of whether they're container or presentation. For functional components, this is just expanding a little bit more on the two, well, three categories that we talked about last time, because we also talked about higher-order components. Functional components are essentially JavaScript functions, and the reason why I say JavaScript functions is, as we talked about when we first started talking about React, a very tricky piece of information on React, when people either describe it as a JavaScript library or as a JavaScript framework. React with TypeScript, but for the most part you're using JavaScript. Functional components are essentially JavaScript functions. They don't necessarily need any data or interaction from another component.

This is an example of a very, very simple functional component in React. You can see that we just do the call for our function, the name of our function, and a very simple statement where we're just asking it to return to us a specific string. We're not passing any user ID. We're not asking it to validate anything. We're not passing data to it. We're just giving it a specific instruction to do something with the information we've given within that section.

Here's another example here now if we use ES6 JavaScript. This is with a class. We have a class Welcome. We're extending it to React. It extends a React component. Its whole purpose is to render some information, and then we ask it to return another string in an H1 heading where we say hello. We ask it to print the name of likely the user that's logging in. We're saying this.props.name. We know that props is a way that we pass on data. In this case, even though we're essentially saying, "Use the value of the name property in this," We already have that within it. Class Components is another type of React Component, and it's very similar to functional components, but they have a few added features to it. This is a very simple example of a Class Component. You can see that we're specifying that we have a class title and extending a component again, asking it to render, so to present something on the Viewport to the user. In this case, it is just a simple "I am" title H1 heading.
Class Components can interact with other components and they can pass some data as well. Then we have the Higher Order Components, which we talked about a little bit last time. These are components that essentially take some input and return the component as the output. Usually, when it comes as the output, it has some added functionality to it. It shouldn't be exactly the same component that you get as an output; you always use them with the purpose of adding something to them.
Now, they can be, as I mentioned the last time, very useful for deploying hardened logic, so logic in which you've tested it in other applications. You know that they relate to features, and are quite common in different applications. Things like, for example, depending on how you structure it, could be:
- the registration process
- the logging process
- purchasing something
- adding a user profile information to a database
A lot of these processes, generally speaking, tend to be pretty similar, pretty much the same. Where things are different is on how much of the data you require from the users. Some applications you might just ask the user: I need a password and a username; that's it, or a password and an email; that's it. In other applications, you might say: I need your name, I need your last name, I need your email, I need your, depending if you're shipping things, I need your address; if you're selling things, I need your credit card information. Depending on the purpose of your application, you might request a lot more data, so how you handle the data would be different, especially when you're adding it to a database warehouse. In general, the process you would go through, validating and inserting the data, would be the same, with some requirements different. If you're asking for credit card information, don't do what Nova Scotia Power did; definitely encrypt the information, encrypt passwords as well. It's highly recommended here; it's all the buzz.
We also have dumb components, and as the name suggests, they're essentially just stateless functional components. They are considered to be more efficient than staple components, and that's because they, as dumb components, don't really need a lot of resources for me from your computer, things like memory or CPU or GPU. They don't need a lot, so this is an example of a dumb component. All it does is just export the essentially the value within a particular function, right, so it's not in itself; it's not doing anything; it's not processing anything, any new information; it's not requesting any information. It's just grabbing a function that's already been created that already does something and just exporting; that's it.
Now, the opposite of a dumb component is a smart component, and these are stateful components, very similar to a Class Component. They're kind of like essentially classes or a class-like or an object-type of component that extends components. Oftentimes, you will use it with `React.Component` or `other.component`, so for example here, this smart component again, we're kind of grabbing the same example as our dumb component here. As my component that extends other component. So we're using one of the two commonly used functions here. And we're asking it to render this same H1 statement. So even though, again, even though you can technically arguable, but this is also not doing anything. It's not really grabbing any information, is just presenting something. Yes. It's not necessarily doing a lot, but the structure of how we're writing it is the structure of how you would write a smart component. Then we have present additional components and we talked about those in the last class. There are stateless functional components that mainly focus on the look and feel of the user interface. A lot of presentational components were essentially receive state from HOC components. Um, say relatively because it's not too too short, but it's a little bit brief compared to other lectures that we've had. Um, so so far we've talked about to restful development practices, which is what we are now and most if not I would say all of the frameworks that we tend to use whether you're using rest or Angular review or Django. Um they're all restful frameworks. Now as I said before you might have some developers that will have a much stronger opinion over the which ones are more restful than others and that's a different discussion because as I mentioned some people are very very um strict on restful principles um but uh in terms of using any of those frameworks there are some best practices that are applicable regardless of the framework you choose to use. So the principles in which you use them or that you're meant to use them are the same and the best practices are the same. Where they differ obviously is just on specific technicalities within the frameworks in terms of whether the they have one-way binding, two-way binding some are just a bit easier in the approach or the options that it gives you to scalability than others. Today I'm going to focus on restful or rest-best practices, and we've kind of touched on a few of those throughout the lectures, so I figured it's always better to kind of have them condense into a single one.
As I mentioned, REST, unlike things like RPC or SOAP, is not a protocol. It's a very much a theoretical approach into how you want to set up your development environment and how you want to approach the development process of an application, rather than on using specific tools.
With that in mind, the best practices also sort of focus on what are some best sort of approaches that we can put into place when we're starting our development of particular applications.
1. Nouns instead of verbs
This specifically focuses on how we use HTTP within the context of REST. We know that we have HTTP responses and requests. We have GET, we have PUT, all of those. With REST, we kind of say that you should use those particular response codes or those particular responses or requests as a noun rather than as a verb.
For example, when we're - and that's really to give more of a readability, more of a contextual sort of background to whatever it is you're asking for. Because if you think about it, if we're saying something like GET Canon cameras, then we can kind of gather from the context of just that request that we're probably going to be getting some results in some Canon cameras for this particular database. That's the approach.
Usually, if we kind of look at it from the opposite point of view, if we're using these particular statuses as verbs rather than nouns, then that's when you kind of run into issues like having a long list of resources without meaningful names at all. That's when we used to have a lot of IDs being added onto search or to queries in particular. Now we see a bit more meaningful URLs being developed because we're starting to see these particular words as a noun, so we start to think of it as within the context of an actual request of an actual sentence meaning that the pattern that we use on those URLs starts to become a bit clearer.
When handling asynchronous tasks for example, so if we have an asynchronous task, here we have starting with a POST request, so we're saying POST FILE where specifying the protocol that we're using, version of it, and we're specifying the URL of the host. Whenever we're doing an asynchronous task, you start with either a POST or GET request depending on what it is you're trying to do. Ensure that whatever requests you make issues a response to you. That can be a response, not only a negative response in case there's a mistake, like a forbidden resource or file does not exist, or anything like that, but also make sure that you have responses to let you know that things have been successful, such as in this case, a 202 status being included in the response. It basically just says, "It's the response saying that it's okay, it's been accepted." Then after that, you can have the client check on the status of that GET request if you need to. And that's the whole point of it. Anytime you have a request, you should have a response. Then, if you can check on the status of that specific request from checking that status of that particular request, so in this case, you're checking the status with a GET request because now you're trying to get that message back once the resource. HDP response, so meaning not getting that HTTP response of that 202 status, but actually being redirected to where the resource that they're requesting is. So, does that mean that the response to this GET request check should be a 301 response, which is always what we use for redirects? Also, make sure that the message itself of that response gives a bit of info for us in terms of what it is that you've done, what stage you're at. This is particularly a good practice if you think about it from a logging perspective. You can kind of keep track of how your requests are being handled, where any issues are if there are any, how your system is sort of communicating with different parts of the system. It's just good overall for logging the processes that you're going through, format and media types.

One of the great things about REST and restful frameworks is that you can use any data format, any media type format as well. That is particularly because it is unlikely that we can say in some cases, it might be true, but it is unlikely that we'll be able to say that a single data format will be suitable for every single situation. But sometimes, there might be newer situations that might arise where one particular data format might not be a good idea.

For example, if you have an application that isn't query-heavy, you could argue that well JSON is the best option for this application, and that'll be the one data type that we use. But if your application then grows to allow for multiple search queries to allow users to, let's say, you have an application that tracks joint stock exchange like blood that stock data, if you're just sort of allowing the user to see current stock data, JSON would be fine because you're just updating as you're getting new data. But if now you have an option where the user can look up what was this value for X stock during this week in 2003, now you're adding queries to it. If your application is going to allow for multiple queries and you kind of expect more users to use that query option, then that's when JSON is probably going to struggle with it. It might still be useful, but you're probably going to start to see a little bit of lagging. You're probably going to start to wonder if JSON is still the right choice, and that's when you're having something else, something like SQL data or something like XML data might be something that you start looking at.

Looking into other data forms, and of course, you have that choice at that moment of do I switch everything to see for data or do I want to just switch that part? Like, do I want to just have a database of historical data and that's where I use my queries, and that's perfectly fine. Again, structuring your code in the right way because you're using a REST flow framework would allow you to use multiple data formats if you want to for specific purposes within your application.

Things to consider, though, is sort of the pros and cons of data. If you have data like JSON, one of the main reasons why it's so popular is that it's lightweight, it's humanly readable, easy to consume. But if you're using something like XML, you can have a lot more detail in your data because it's a lot more verbose than JSON. But it is very difficult to read, so it's machine-readable but not necessarily human-readable. You can eventually read it but after a lot of headaches and a lot of time. So definitely consider that. Think about where you're going to be using specific data for what purpose. And what's the best option for you to use, knowing that restful frameworks allow you to use multiple data formats. You don't have to necessarily stick to one.

If you're using caching, caching is built-in to HTTP, which means that it already allows for caching without necessarily coding up to later iterations. That's one of the reasons why we can backtrack within our browser whenever we're clicking. On our browser. This is still the same in terms of whether you can cache on the client, on the server, or proxy server between the client and the server.
The one thing to keep in mind, though, is that POST requests are not considered cacheable. Now they're not considered it's up to you to decide for the most part when we're using POST requests. These tend to be associated with more sensitive information.
So examples of POST requests: a logging form, registration form, payment form. By definition, you're basically going through a POST request because you want to add an extra layer of security to that request to those messages being sent to the server.
So if you're trying to cache POST requests, you have to really ask yourself: should you?
1. Do you have any sensitive information that you need to consider and maybe not go the route of caching?
2. Are you okay with the potential hit you're going to get on performance if you start caching POST requests?
That would be the biggest thing on on posts on caching POST requests. They can lead on the client side to slow performance and, as I said, because of the sensitive data that we tend to associate with POST requests, security risks. Can you? You can't. Should you? That's the question.
Security for REST. Are RESTful frameworks 100% safe? We still have a lot of work to do. Yes, they make the starting point for our applications easier, faster. They can allow us to make for more efficient and scalable applications, but the security part we still have a lot of work to do.
In terms of that, so any kind of servers that you might be creating with RESTful frameworks, we have to be smart and still think about it the same way as we always do with any particular application. We should require authentication for accessing any sort of sensitive resources: user profile information, payment history, things like that. If you have an unauthenticated user trying to get a resource that they should not guess, then obviously that's where the response is coming. Having those meaningful responses being given to the user in this case trying to access a resource that they don't have access to would be a 401 response code.
Other things as well to keep in mind with RESTful best practices:
1. Use HTTPS instead of HTTP
2. Try to add a hash message authentication code instead of digest authentication, or a much more robust option
There are different things in terms of security. This is the one part that is developing even more so than I would say at the moment. RESTful frameworks and it's partly because of AI, because of how much more polished and sophisticated those web attacks tend to get.
So definitely try to go for the most robust options that you can to ensure security while still being mindful of performance. Remember, the more security you add or layers you add to an application, the more likely that application is going to take a little bit of a performance hit. So you have to hit that kind of sweet spot where you're happy with how it's performing. Or use something like Open Authentication or different options as I mentioned. So in this lecture I kind of I'm grouping everything together then we'll go specifically into the restful principles and then we're going to go into the different frameworks itself. So for service oriented architecture and in the pre-recorded videos I always do a pre-recorded video on SOAP and RPC as well and even though I'm hoping now nobody uses RPC or SOAP I do think it's important to understand what what led us to the restful frameworks that we're using today like what were the pain points that got people to go from RPC to SOAP and then from SOAP to SOA right? And perhaps the biggest difference between RPC and SOAP particularly SOAP and SOA and RES is that SOAP is a protocol. SOA is sort of a theoretical view of how your architecture should be set up and RES is more of a theoretical view on programming practices that sort of mimic service oriented architecture principles as well but they're particularly geared towards how we program something when we're using restful services or restful frameworks like React and Angular and Vue and all of those.

So for I'll start with service oriented architecture so for SOA services the main purpose of this was how do we come up with applications that are easier to maintain? That are easier to develop? That are easier to scale? Because now it's where you know we start to see quite the boom in terms of the different types of applications that are available you know Facebook is probably one of the examples that most clearly marks where there was a pretty big change in web applications to becoming more service oriented whatever that service may be to the user not necessarily to the client necessarily thinking about it from the perspective of their developer but now that we start thinking about that now we have to start thinking about how do how do we structure this how do we make it so that we're you know not spending 80 percent of our time fixing and that's before AI fixing our own code now it's more like 60. Although I did see a report that actually says that's going up now because of AI to 75% because of how AI codes things right so in terms of service oriented architecture whenever we think of it we can kind of assume that specific within an application, we've thought about few elements such as:
1. Process
2. People
3. Practice
4. Platforms
For process, it essentially means aligning our tech with the business operations, with how things are meant to work in terms of practice, making sure we use best practices. Platform primarily focuses on efficiency and this is the part where scalability becomes quite important. That idea that whatever application we build with SOA, we have to assume it is likely to grow and therefore that process has to be thought about from the very beginning so that we can structure our code to make it easier to scale.
And then people primarily if we're coming up with services that are meant to complement the work that people do or meant to facilitate particular processes, then we do need to include those decision makers into that process.
Now there's three key roles or three rules specifically in every service-oriented architecture building block whenever we're thinking about our services within our applications:
1. Service provider
2. Registry
3. Service consumer (also sometimes called the service requester)
For the service provider, I'll move my little cursor down here. So for the service provider, basically the provider works with the registry to determine how a service is to be offered, any security considerations, any information in terms of availability as the service is unavailable because it's currently fulfilling another request, all of those details between those two.
And then we have the service registry which is essentially a registry. It makes service-related information available to those who want to request the service. In this case, the service consumer or requester, and then the service consumer or requester essentially tries to locate the entry on a particular service within the registry and then binds them to the service provider.
Now if we think about how we build applications, we essentially think of it in terms of:
- What process are we trying to facilitate?
- What sort of assumptions can we make or what prerequisites have to be fulfilled in order for somebody to be able to request a particular service?
- What makes that service available?
So for example, logging in - if you think of it, you're just requesting a particular process. To be able to use an application, there are certain conditions that will have to be fulfilled. The user has to be registered, the user during the registration has to have fulfilled certain requirements. If we have a service where we have different categories of users, then now we have extra information that determines what that particular user has access to based on the role that they play in a particular organization.
Now this was done with the main purpose of simplifying applications out there, and I'll show you a little snippet of this exact same application but without SOA applied to it.
But some key principles in terms of SOA again to allow for applications to become scalable, maintainable, and therefore more efficient is to structure them in a way that you know and encourages reusing code. In the sense that if you come up with a particular module and it works well, and you have a different application that needs to reuse that module, that should be something that's fairly easy to do using multiple languages. Before SOA, we used to have applications that had to be built in a single language. Now we have layers, now we have stacks. We can use a Python framework like Flask which we are going to do in this semester, and I'm excited for that! We can framework for the front end, we can have Flask and Node work together, so Python and JavaScript, or if you want, you can also use a PHP-based framework or even Spring Boot if you really wanted to use a Java-based framework. But you're not tied to just that one language. You can layer as much as you want. Again thinking in terms of how Facebook has grown they've gone from having their application be based on LAMP so that's Linux Apache right MySQL and PHP to now having an application still based on LAMP but now adding React to it now adding a bunch of other frameworks that are SQL-based frameworks. So that idea that you have to completely switch things sometimes it makes sense but sometimes you have an application that's so big that you just have to think about how do I layer other things here to make it more efficient knowing that the alternative which is to start from scratch is probably not the right option with sort of applications that go from this example on the left which is quite if we just think of just look at the colors and the lines it's kind of complicated right. When you can see right away that we have some repeated services that are actually coded within specific like specific components they're not modules are reused so for example we have order status via part of account management and then we have another order status that was also coded within order processing then we have check customer status which we have in service scheduling and then also in order processing same thing for determined product availability both as order and service and then we see how all the data is just going through a bunch of different repos a bunch of different databases and just looking at this graph on the left we can already see if one thing goes wrong everything goes down there doesn't matter what it is right because we can kind of make the assumption that this is the structure of a system that's very tightly coupled that has lots of dependencies probably. So if one thing goes wrong it is likely to cause sort of a domino effect on on other things but now when we think about SOA if we grab that same type of service and we just apply some SOA principles and we just looked at it from the perspective of what would that architecture look like? If we wanted to just do a graph on it we first of all we would identify what are some of the services that really should be modules that could be reused so we have the create invoice check customer status check order status check credit check inventory we can come up with as many other services as well and then we automatically know that you know in the case of for example order processing and service schedule both of them use the same check customer status module. We're not repeating our code, we're just reusing the module. We're making it available to those two types of applications. Same thing with check order status between order processing and account management. Automatically we start to see well this is more simple because if I need to you know the obvious thing I need to make a change to one of the services I know that it's going to affect these other applications it's just going to simplify how those updates happen and in terms of how the data is then sent to our data repository the process is also simplified because of how our business services are going to be sending the information right based on whether it's related to customer customers related to orders related to the service itself related to the company itself. So now what does that mean for APIs and REST? So REST will for the most part or REST for the most part tends to provide the service layer. Thanks to Fall of 2005, when REST comes onto the map. This is also when we start to think about not necessarily web services, but web APIs, because of that idea of reusability, maintainability, and scalability.

Now, one quick example of that is if we think about banks, just how services or online banking have evolved probably in the last ten years. Now with web APIs and this is how APIs start to just become such a noticeable sort of adoption or transition into RESTful architectures and RESTful services is that web APIs usually refer to services that expose data through HTTPS, so through web requests and web responses. In other words, if we wanted to think about it so far, we've kind of talked about HTTPS requests. We know that we make a request or a response every time that the user triggers some kind of an action or we respond to some sort of an action. But if we start to go a little bit more in-depth, then now we start to think about how that data gets exposed.

So REST is supposed to mimic as much as possible how the web works when you're creating your applications. Meaning when we think of the World Wide Web is a very loosely coupled system. You can access any page you want; an interaction on one website does not affect another website you're accessing or not accessing a page does not affect how you act or access or not access other pages. Very loosely coupled. You can add pages, you can create your own website and add it, and you don't have to worry about what's out there already. That's what I mean by it. And in terms of REST, REST kind of believes in that principle that your service should, in theory, work the same way as the World Wide Web within the context of only your service. It should not depend on anything else. Right, you can decide to register, but if you decide not to register, you can still access the website. There may be some things you can't access because you haven't been granted permission because you haven't fulfilled the condition of registering, but it doesn't mean that because you haven't registered you can't access anything.

Now, in terms of RAS and web APIs, for the most part, they tend to use JSON as their data format. It does not, notice that I said for the most part they tend to. They do not only use JSON. You can use XML data if you want as your main format. You can even use text (plain text) as your data format. You can use images, or literally use any format that you want. But more often than not, you tend to see RESTful frameworks and web APIs opting for JSON.

Now, with RESTful frameworks, because they're meant to be flexible, any RESTful framework should allow you to use any data format that you want. RESTful web APIs should also allow you to use HTTP restfully. What does that mean? So, using HTTP restfully means that you're adhering to REST constraints over HTTP. We will have a lecture on what those constraints actually are because we do have architectural constraints and we do have principles as well, and they're both connected to each other, but they're not interchangeable necessarily. Making sure that you correctly, accurately, properly use HTTP methods for your CRUD transactions. So, if you need to update something, you're using the correct HTTP method for it. If you need to delete something or create something, right, maybe we're using those proper methods that we don't just decide, "Well, I'm just going to use create for everything, even if I'm just updating something. I'm just going to use create." Right, so with REST, it's there quite strict principles because, in their nature, they're flexible, but they're very specific in how your code, in your work, is implemented.

Implementing secure transfer for any data that you need to exchange between your client and your server. That does mean that, to have something that you claim to be RESTful, the assumption is you are using HTTPS. If you use HTTP, you shouldn't say that you have something that's RESTful.

Now, as I say that, and when we get to the lecture on RESTful principles, I will also tell you that you can follow all of these principles to a tee, and you will still meet one person that will say your application is not RESTful. And that's just because those who are very into the principles are very specific as to how you need to use certain things. So, it is possible for you to always meet somebody that tells you what you're doing is not fully RESTful. Don't think that, in any way, means that you don't actually have a RESTful system. Often times, it's just opinions. Just do your best to follow as much as you can the principles. Which, for the most part, if you're using a RESTful framework, the frameworks are structuring a way that facilitates you sticking to those principles. And then, RESTful E is also to use key components of RESTful HTTPS requests to make sure you use them not only properly, but make sure that you use them.

So, what I mean by that is to make sure that you use URLs to make sure that your URLs are structured in a RESTful manner, which is very specific as well. You can also, if you look at some literature, they might instead of using URLs, actually if you're working with web APIs and you have had any issues accessing your web API (meaning you can access it or you can actually make the requests to it), often times you would have gotten an error that was an endpoint error. Endpoint and URL are the same thing. So, using URLs or endpoints in the the correct particles. So if you're doing a web request, it has to use HTTP. If you're using a file transfer request, you have to use HTTPS as well. Making sure that the protocol you're using matches what the request is meant to do and that that protocol is attached to an endpoint that follows the correct REST format. Making sure you use the correct method, which goes with the second point of properly using HTTP methods. So whether you're using create, update, delete, for example. And then specifically with your HTTP messages, making sure that they follow the proper format. Every HTTP message has a set of headers. The header describes information on if it's a response, who made the request, what they requested for, and then what your response is and where is the response coming from? It'll include any information in terms of HTTP status codes. Was the request successful? Is it getting an OK message? Was the file not found? All of those things where we usually see that HTTP 200s, 300s, 400s, and 500 range messages. They should indicate something. And then the message itself within the body should be descriptive of what happened. Sometimes these HTTP messages will go between the client and the server. But a lot of times when we're working with RESTful APIs and RESTful frameworks, we have to consider the fact that we want to make sure we're sending meaningful messages between our services because if something goes wrong, we have to be able to debug it. And more often than not the only time or the only information we have to be able to properly debug something is when we leave crumbs for ourselves. Right? This didn't work and then we know specifically where to go because we've defined what the disk was. Which technically means if you had somebody else working on a similar industry, they might actually come up with something different. In the past few weeks, we've focused on Front-End Design and Development Techniques, we've discussed UI and UX Design techniques and even began to venture on exploring different Front-End Development Frameworks. As developers, it's easy for us for focus on how applications work rather than how they look or encourage interaction and part of that is because Front-End development has evolved quite a bit over the last few decades . . . even more so the last 5 years!

Today I'm going to talk a little bit about mistakes in web development. One of the biggest mistakes, I would say, is amongst web developers is foregoing the user. Now, there are other mistakes that we also make, some applicable, some privacy-related, but a lot of times, more often than not, it tends to do over-the-counter.

Now, sometimes when I say "user," I might be talking about end-user, that is, who is using the actual application. Sometimes the user might be an employee. So maybe you're developing an application for a government agency, and that your user would be a government employee who would then use the application to provide a service to what we would normally call an end-user. Okay?

So, I'm going to go across to the service. Important things that often times get overlooked, that always come into play when we're trying to understand the user are things like wireframing, the planning of colour palette, specifically colour palette. I do find that a lot of times developers tend to, if you're not working with a design, you tend to go with whatever colour palette is popular at the time or suggested by somebody at the time, and sometimes that might work. But you also have to ask yourself what kind of message you're conveying with it. For example, if you're building an application for a bank, more often than not, those banks tend to use blue in their applications in some way, shape or form, and the reason for that is because blue often gets associated with trust. What's the one thing we feel when we go to a bank? When you have a check that needs to deposit, and when you're living on the edge and you don't want to use the ATM, you have to move it across between your deep. Eventually you have to hand over the check to a perfect stranger and trust that they're going to put it in your account right? And then you walk away and the first thing you do is double-check: Yes, I do want to receive it? Yes, I do want it? And then you still go to your online bank and make sure that it's there and double-check everything right? But that's one of the reasons why they tend to use blue. Now, this does kind of change based on bank, based on the geographic location, based on cultural histories. For example, I come from Alberta, and for a long period of time, I'm going to say roughly now too, we're not necessarily known for the most stable banking system. In fact, we had an event that was called Bankruptcy of the State where about 20 banks went bankrupt on the same day at the same time. I think they estimated it was definitely 98% of the population if not 100% of the population had limited or no access to funds whatsoever because they were also going to pay for it.

Since that event, not only do you see banks using the water, but you also see every single application and all the advertisements plastered with pictures of people that worked. Because they know that people still remember that event even though it happened in 2000. People still remember that. There are some people that are still getting their money back from the banking agencies. So it's a long process right?

It can mean a lot more than you think, so you always have to go back to one of the things I always mentioned to young developers is you have to have an idea. You have to stay flexible.

Because other times when we build something, we build something because we think it's going to be useful because, well, I use this application this way, it should work that way, so I'm going to build something new that works exactly how I wish. Those are really important for particularly for how we use an application for specific features of an application. So we have six specific principles:
1. Good Figure principle where you can have objects grouped together based on something.
2. You can have similarity which is similar items grouped together.
3. Maybe you've defined all these items in these circles so that's how you decide.gestalt principles between items. Closure, which is also creating that visual connection between sets of elements. We have here the wildlife non-profit logo as well, and this is a good example of closure. And this is basically when you have different shapes In this case, you have different shapes working together to show as if you have a closed item or a closed handle, where you actually don't have the closed shape, right? So we have one leg here, but that leg is not connected to this ear. We have another ear here. That ear is connected to the shape here of the other leg. Now when I talk about user experience, sometimes the term can be used in conjunction with usability and they are related but they're not the same. So that's something key. How long does it take someone to learn from you? It is just whatever measure we want to take when defining ease of use. The user experience on the other hand is the sum of all series of interactions that a user can have with a navigation or a service keeping in mind that those interactions could be online, offline or online too. Right? So let's say that the low-fidelity prototype on the sheet of paper takes you, let's say, five minutes to do just one script. Not a lot. So it just takes you one for one of the screens. It takes five minutes, and then in your prototype, let's say it takes you 10 hours because you really get into it, so you take about a full day of work. Then you show your work to a colleague, group member, friend, or boss, and your boss says, "Dave, will really like it. They want you to change pretty much everything." Which of the two approaches is going to make you more receptive to that feedback? The one you spend five minutes on or the one you spent a whole day on? You never get back, right?

So that's what prototyping comes in. It's a quick thing. The whole purpose of it is we need to get feedback. So you don't want to spend too much time because, as developers, the longer we spend on something, the more protected that something is. So the less likely we're going to be open to negative feedback. And more often than not, we will get some negative feedback. Sometimes more than others, but we actually want that. We want that constructive criticism where somebody says, "This is too small for me, but it's not for all of the users," so you can start to think about okay, so this element has to be respected. This is how you respond to it, so you can adjust to a particular user. Right? So that's where low-fidelity prototypes come in. That's when you also see them as basic as you see them here. It kind of makes the focus be a layout and the interaction rather than what it looks like because there's no colors, there's no flashing, and so it already sways the feedback to be more about how it could function, how it could be used rather than "I don't want that color! I don't want that shadow!" This is where we want to use things like a group of feet or a pig mind. Maybe spend a little bit more time in the potential book of the prototype. But, again, if you're doing things like that, do keep in mind that they're sitting here. So you want to make sure that you stay open to that feedback. Don't spend too much time on specific things. Because what you want is to get that feedback to then actually come to the development of the application.

Also keep in mind that the more design elements you implement (like in this example), the more likely your feedback will sway towards the aesthetics of the application. So if you're looking for interaction and this would be the type of product I would use. Because automatically, first thing it shows somebody says, "What do you think?" They'll immediately say, "Oh, I like the color" or "I don't know about the yellow." Right? They'll immediately go to the aesthetics of it.

Whereas if you show an example like this or like this, they're not going to talk about it because there's no functionality. So they're more likely going to see, "Okay, I can kind of see how these are different screens and how you're moving from one to the other," and then you can get some feedback.

Which type of prototyping option do I choose? Depends on the type of effect. If it's more interaction, more layout, a low-fidelity prototype is more suitable. If it's more in terms of design elements and aesthetics, an in-between or high-fidelity prototype would be more suitable. A high-fidelity prototype has a bit of function that we've so that's the one with low-fidelity mainly kind of wireframing. No functionality at all whatsoever. And in-between still no functionality, some design elements added to it so it makes a little sense for what the finished product might look like. And then a high-fidelity product gives you it's much closely related to that final product and it has some functionality so you can get a sense of how it should, how the application is going to look. There's an obvious interpretation of this. You don't want it to be displayed in your app. Also, when you package your code and you provide it to someone and have it installed on their device, your code is available to that user if they know how to unpack it and look at the code. You don't really want to have those API keys stored on someone's device as part of your packaged code. To do this in a better way, you could have these keys stored somewhere on a server and then have your app dynamically retrieve these keys so that it's never actually on the user's device when they look at their code to unpack it. Things like AWS Secrets Manager, Google Cloud Security Manager, Azure Key Vault, there are lots of different ways that you can securely store your keys on a server and then retrieve them dynamically during runtime.  That was the NFC. The underlying text RFID? Is it RFID? It's similar to RFID. We'll talk about the differences. The main difference is the one-way versus two-way communication, but this includes that short-range contact, similar to RFID. Things like your tap-to-pay, scanning a bus pass, and all these kinds of short-range callbacks within an SBA. That's the actual stance for your communication, so that kind of gives us that short-range thing there. It generally works up to 10 cm, but usually less. You can think about the instances where you use a torch, and those 10 cm won't work. It has some raised RFID, which is radio frequency identification, but NFC also has two-way communication, which could make a change in the data. For example, while RFID can send information, it wouldn't be able to send and receive. When you need to make a payment, you need to send some information and also receive information back.

These tags have no power source. When they enter the radius of their field, they're powered momentarily. That's how that short-range communication works. When you take the device away again, the NFC tag is no longer powered. Once they're within that same electromagnetic field, the communication can occur in both directions.

There are two types of NFC tags: passive and active. Passive tags are things that you want to stick on everything; they're cheaper and simple for short-range applications where low power consumption is key. For example, an ID card, your student ID, for example, has a passive NFC tag in it. You'd love to scan something; it's momentarily powered by the reader. Active tags are more complex; they have a longer range and more functionality. They're used in things like tapping, monitoring smart devices. These would be more expensive and maybe more useful in particular applications, but generally a passive NFC tag is kind of a small issue as well.

I mentioned we've used a lot of different things like bus passes, access cards, and many more. You can check out this short if you're interested to see what kind of data you can be getting. Is it going to be text or potentially other data, and then you have your payload, which is the actual content that you're sending? I think your type here is text, and then your content is a string of information, and then you have a header. To use NFC in reactivative, we can use the reactivative ns this is where you can find a lot of specific permissions. Once you have that file generated, you can go ahead and add in these two permissions for your team:
1. A user's permission tag. This is how you find that you're going to use this permission, and if it is necessary, you can go ahead and do it. You don't want this tag to be related to errors.
2. A user's feature tag. This one is optional, but if you're planning to publish your app to the app store, it will help your app only show up for devices that have that functionality. Ideally, when a user is going through the app store, they only want to see apps where they would actually be able to install and use them. If their device doesn't have that activity in their use, this app will just show up for them. It's kind of like an alternative here anyway.

The idea of data persistence is the sense of maintaining data between different states of life. Essentially keeping data persistent through different screens and states. Perhaps this is something like some sort of form input that the user will enter or preferences that they'll select that you want to be persistent even once you move away.

The importance of this is that prevents loss of important user data (e.g. app references), it would help to ensure smoother user experience, safe form inputs (e.g. if they get sent to another stream and maintain some access). It also defines what lines support the user when they're not able to continuously download new data.

This is the overall idea of data persistence, but there's a lot that goes into this and a lot of different forms of persistence that we can look at.

To kind of illustrate this through an example, if we imagine we have an email app (like an app where users ideally sign in, send some emails, receive some emails or ask for other emails), what kind of data would we be storing on an app like this? Any thoughts on how to keep consistent? Emails? And then for each email, what are we storing? Subtracted content, meta-data. Like attachments and media stuff? Logged in user data. Your actual information, email, and also session tokens? Yeah, so that the actual login information that needs to be in the password. Well, I don't know if you'd be storing the password necessarily, but we don't store the password. I don't know if the user has entered the password correctly. With magic or token encryption stuff? Yeah, so it'll be encrypted, you can check the flag. We have something like shared preferences. Yeah, no, there's a concept like shared preferences that stores flags and you know just key-value pairs that if the user is logged in or not it checks the states. Like the user's username so that when they move after the login screen into the email view, you can see "Hello user" or something like this. This is just a very simple example, and then we get into more complex data like the email itself. We see a lot of things underneath the email where having more structure to the way that we store this could be helpful.

For example, we don't just want to have a list of subject lines and then a separate list of senders and receivers and then a separate list of content. We want these to be interrelated in some way so that we can tell which subject lines are linked to which email, or which sender, which attachments relate to that specific email. They're kind of interrelated in that way.

And then we get into the idea of file attachments, which, while they fit into that same structure, we also want them to have as a device itself. So you would be able to download the attachment and save it in the file structure of the device, which is a bit different from the way that the rest of the emails have.

One thing about user preferences: we have these different approaches to data persistence that can help us with these different needs. For example, when this simple string type data (like the user's username) is needed so that you can refer to that same username on different screens, we can use key-value storage. We'll use async storage for this.

This is what's meant for example in your project description where it says "simple data". It's the kind of data we're looking at today. So it's simple, but it has all these different types of data for storing, but also it helps with queries. If we want to now retrieve say an email from a specific sender or retrieve a list of emails we received from a specific sender, we will be able to query this using this row and column structure. This will be familiar to a lot of you from previous database classes, but we'll definitely do a question on it as well. It's also good for supporting large databases because of the way you query them. Combining these last two approaches could be a good idea if your databases are very large and use the types you use.

The last approach is cloud storage. This is going to be a very common one that you'll see nowadays where the data will be stored on the local device, and the data will be available to the users when they change devices or if they want to communicate with other users (for example, sending an email to somebody else and then seeing it in web view vs. mobile view). This is best for reducing the data stored locally, so saving some of those resources. But unless you tie it with something like a database where you query things, it can be difficult to figure out what to download to the local device when needed.

A lot of different approaches we can use, and there are probably others that I won't get into here. Using these four approaches can be a lot of fun. Okay, great. So this is SQL-like for those who haven't done it before. It's a language for managing data in relational databases. It's very similar to SQL and each app has its own private SQL-like database, so different apps won't be able to access different databases. Self-designed transaction-based, and by transaction-based I mean that you can add things to the database and also query them at a pretty decent pace. And it doesn't require a server because it's locally stored. So this is where the limitation of the SAP Data Agents comes in, but it's really great for handling complex data. It is locally stored, so having large databases quickly adds up and takes up a lot of space. and, uh can impact the performance of your application. Um and that's where cloud storage comes in.

So we have each of these approaches has its pros for the database. So this is going to be things like the database name, the version, the different tables that we have within the database, and the column names within those tables. Just general information about your database as well.

Then once we have that schema in place, we go ahead and create the database. We have a series of queries that can do that for us. And then we can execute queries to insert, update, delete, or retrieve data in different ways.

So for database schema (like I said), it defines how our data is organized and how data has relationships with one another. And what I mean by that is that for example, you might have two different tables: one table for storing shopping list items in a store, and another table that is going to show purchase histories of different users or something like this. And those purchase histories are probably going to make references to shopping items that exist in that store. So there's going to be relationships between the different tables within that dataset.

So within it, there are tables which are going to represent large structures of specific types of data or you know, you have rows and columns that will maintain the same structure across records. So each row is going to be an individual record or an individual entry in that table, and the columns are going to be keys essentially. So you kind of have the same key-value structure as we've seen before where the keys are not a column and the value exists in the other column. In database name. In order to access the database as a child of this SQLite provider, we have to use the useSqliteContext hook. And then we can use db for the rest of this part of the host. Okay, so now the next thing that we want to use is execAsync, which we saw for creating a table, but also runs asynchronously. There's more that these can do besides just create the table. So db again is the instance of our database connection. Both of these methods, both runAsync and execAsync, send SQL queries to the database, so SQL is the language that's being used here. SQL is the implementation of that language in this context. Both of these are asynchronous functions; neither of them will block the thread; they'll each execute the SQL statement on a separate thread.

runAsync is useful when you want to execute write operations (e.g., insert, update, delete), but execAsync is a little bit broader than just those. So, really, any SQL statement can be passed into execAsync and the difference is that it returns a promise which results with the result. So, the result is going to give us some details about what's returned from our SQL query. So, if you wanted to run read queries, execAsync would be the better place to do it because we want to then be able to process the result in some way. Does that make sense? Any questions so far? So, what we want our app to do is to write to a database and also to read from the database in order to display the results as part of a list that we can then edit or delete or clear.

The way that we would read from our database, in this case, we don't really want to filter in any particular way. We just want to get all of the notes that we save to the database. We can use the select statement from SQL where we say "select* from my_notes", which is the name of our table. What this does is it's going to select all entries from the my_notes table, so the asterisk kind of just means anything could go here, so we're selecting everything from my_notes and then we're going to display that in our note array which comes from the USE_STATE hook here.

So, what we're doing is we're defining a note_r state. We have a setter for it, set_note_r, and we're initializing it to an empty array. Then, we are going to inside useEffect create an asynchronous function that we call fetchData, which is going to be responsible for fetching data from our SQLite database. Inside that, we're going to call getAllAsync. There's lots of different functions that come from the database implementation here, getAllAsync is one of them. We could have also used execAsync, either one would work. Then, we again pass in our SQL statement here, "select* from my_notes", and we get back a result from this function call. So there are a lot of different approaches to how you can do something like this, but this is a very straight-forward one. This is also an asynchronous function. We're not taking in any parameters. And we're going to execute using execAsync the statement "drop table if exists minots." So this is going to check to see whether a table exists called minots and if it does exist we're going to drop it or delete it from the database. That means it no longer exists at all, and that would be one sort of table dropped successfully.

Then the next thing we're going to do because now we've dropped the table entirely and the rest of our app isn't really going to function anymore because it makes a lot of references to minot. So if we've dropped it now we won't be able to add anything to it in the future. So what we want to do is kind of make sure that doesn't happen by immediately creating a new table with the same specifications after we've dropped the previous one. This is just resetting all of those table entries including everything that existed but still recreating the structure so we can continually use the database.

So we're going to use execAsync again and we're going to execute the statement "create table if not exists minots." We're checking to see if there isn't already a table minots. There shouldn't be but still just in case we're checking. And then we're going to create this table again with the same columns. So we have an id that's an integer, it's a primary key, it's auto-incremented, and then we're creating another column that is a column note and type is text. And then we're logging into our console, the table is recreated and the ID counter is reset. So the ID counter being reset here is important to keep in mind because once we've cleared that previous table, all of our auto-incremented IDs are gone as well, and we're starting again from scratch. Okay, some of you have indicated that you would like to learn more about the product and why it works. Okay, so how do we make custom animations, things that go beyond just those functions that we looked at in our last class?

One of the approaches that comes from the actual field of animation is key framing. The idea of key framing is that historically, when animations were like, think about old Disney animations. Your hands draw animations quite smoothly, even though they are literally frame-by-frame pictures.

The way that they would do this in animation studios is that they would hire different, let's say, skill levels of artists. You'd have one animation artist who was the very talented animation artist in the studio, and then you'd have a larger number of other animation artists. You give that lead animator what you call these key frames, which are the key moments in the movement or the animation sequence. In between these key frames, we have what's called an in-between, which are all the less important frames.

You might have noticed, when you watch these old animations, especially maybe lower quality ones, you'll kind of see slight shifts in style between the different frames. That's because there were different artists there, but we still have the ability to follow the structure of it because those key frames are typically done very well. Those kind of mark the more extreme points in motion. If a ball is bouncing up and then down, the down position and the up position are going to be a key frame, so then everything in between is kind of interpolated between those positions.

Nowadays we don't really have hand-drawn animations as much, but in digital animation, if you're doing things with Maya or, let's say, Blender or something like that, or even video editing, you'll often see video editing sequences where you use key frames. All you do with these cases is to find what happens at those more extreme points in motion, and then the in-betweens are automatically interpolated by calculations done by the software. It makes things a lot smoother, of course, in digital animation, but the same concept still kind of carries through. Does this make sense?

Okay, so we can now use the same idea. Again, if we kind of look at this as meaning an animation sequence, we have a number of different frames of this animation where a ball is bouncing down and then up and then down and up and down, and then up and down. Our key frames here would be that very first frame where the ball is at location zero. It starts at the top there; it's round, and then we come down to the next key frame, which is frame 1, 2, 3, 4, 7. There's 7; that is our key frame there, and we see it at the lower position. The ball is transformed, so it's scaled across the x and y axes, so its coordinates as well as transformations can change to these key frames. We see it move to the next one, but in between these we see kind of these in-between states where it's maybe slightly stretched, less and less stretched in these different locations, based on what frames are between the keyframes.

We want to apply this concept now to mobile animation. We again use the reanimated API, and in this case we can kind of do the same thing that we're doing in our previous examples, where we define these transformations. For example, we were using these transformations in our useAnimatedState calls that we were building, but this time instead of using just one transform that caused the entire animation, it was quite how the transformations but then all these are zero and 100, so zero is going to be the beginning of our animation and 100 is going to be the end of our animation. Does this mean that the numbers represent the actual frames? No, right, because not all animations have just 500 frames. In fact, most mobile applications would be rendering many, many, many frames per second, and so 100 frames would go up really quickly. This is kind of a percentage view of the same idea, though.

If you look at, like, 45, that's like 45% of the way through whatever the duration of that animation is. Of course, we can define duration with a number of these different functions we reviewed in our last class. For this example on the side here, we defined a new keyframe, so keyframes coming from the reanimated API. Keyframes 0, 45, and 100 were defining a few different transformations. If you look at what's happening, we're transforming at frame zero to a rotation of 0 degrees, at frame 45 (45% of the way through animation) we're transforming it to rotate 100 degrees using the exponential easing function, and then at frame 100 (100% of the way through our animation) we're transforming by rotating to 45 degrees. What's going to happen is we're going to rotate forward and rotate slightly backward.

We are using this keyframe the same way we use any of our other animations by passing it into the animation props of our animated component. In this case, we're using animated.view, which is an animated version of view, so it has this entering prop where we can define its entering animation. We could just simply have done this through using exiting animation or by using shared values to dynamically change this animation without it being an enter or exit animation.

Let's take a look at an example of this. Any questions? Okay, let me make this bigger. Here we have this keyframe animation. We have React and useState coming from React. We have view button stylesheet and pressable coming from React Native. We have animated keyframe and easing coming from React Native.

First thing we're doing in this keyframe example is we're defining a state. So useState is starting off with true; we have our state visible and set visible. What happens when visible is changed in terms of animation and rendering? Will this affect anything, like a re-render? Exactly when a state changes, we always re-render. This is why there's a benefit to using shared values in a lot of cases, because the shared value, when it changes, it doesn't trigger a re-render, so you can continue smooth animation. In this case, we kind of want that behavior where changing the state will manually cause it to re-render whenever we want.

When you find a custom animation using the keyframe here, and keyframe is again coming from React and reanimated for column entry animation at zero, so the start of our animation, we're starting off with an opacity of 1. Again, these are coming from the same things that we use with our useAnimated state, the same props that we can alter here. Opacity of 1 means that it's opaque, right, so there's no transparency to it at frame zero. At our trip, then we're transforming it here so that we set up with a y translation of -50. We're going to rotate to 20 degrees and we'll scale it at 1, which means that it's the original size of whatever component we're applying this to. Scale of 1 means a 10% scale. Rotation to 20 degrees means slightly tilted to the right, and translation of -50 on the y-axis is going to be relatively further up than the origin. At 50, so part way through, half way through exactly the animation, we're going to set your half to 0, then we transform it to -85. Which way is it going to move? From 0 to 50, what direction is it going to move in? This is:
- a negative move up, positive move down on the x-axis
- a negative move left, positive move right
We're moving up between frame 0 and 50, and we're changing the opacity from 1 to 0. We're also rotating to negative 180 degrees. After turning off total study to the right, we're going to move in the opposite direction of 180 degrees, and we'll scale it to 0.5. We're changing slightly between frame 0 and 50. We have a function here, so the movement between frames 0 and 50 isn't necessarily going to be exactly linear, and the changes between frames 0 and 50 aren't going to be exactly linear. It's going to slightly ease towards these values. That's going to affect the interpolation that occurs automatically and is being done for us by the software here.
At 100, at the end of the animation, we're going to set the opacity back to 1 and we're going to transform it by setting the translate Y to 0. After we moved up to maybe 5, we're now going to move down all the way to 0. We'll set the rotation to 0 degrees, so we're kind of rotating right-left and then to 0, and we're setting scale back to 1.
How do we know how long our animation is? Well, we've set these different rates, but we don't know how long it is at this point until we set the duration of the animation. Here I'm resetting it to 1000 ms or 1 s, so across 1 s we're going to make a number of changes at these keyframes. If we want to, we can define way more keyframes within this animation and create a more complex animation with a number of different changes within it, but this is just an example where it's pretty straightforward.
We have our exiting animation here. We have another keyframe being defined. This time we have four keyframes that we are setting:
- At frame 0, we're setting a pass rate of 1.
- We're rotating X to 0 degrees.
- At frame 10, we're setting a pass rate of 1 again, so it's still maintaining that fully opaque nature.
- We're transforming it by rotating by 10 degrees, and we're using the exponential easing there as well, like a sweaty flap.
- Then at 100, so we end our animation, the opacity is 0, so it's gone from fully opaque to fully transparent.
- We're transforming it by setting the rotation to 0 degrees, and we're again setting the duration to 1 s.
We have an entering animation and an exiting animation defined, but they're not being used anywhere until we explicitly use them ourselves. Here in the JSX, we're exporting a view, just the typical view like what you've seen before. We're using the state visible in our new state to toggle whether we can see these things that are inside this here, so we're using the logical and. If visible is true, we're going to return an animated view that has the entering and exiting animation, and inside this, the invisible, that's going to affect the kind of toggle or visible state.
The purpose of this is that when we have this pressable, this button essentially pressed, and we toggle from visible to not visible, what happens is that our view is going to exit. If it's no longer visible, it's kind of removed from this JSX hierarchy, so we'll be able to trigger that exiting animation manually. When we toggle the state back to visible, then it's going to be re-added to our JSX hierarchy, so we can kind of manually trigger that entering animation. Does that make sense?
Entering is when something is added to our layout, and exiting is when something is removed from our layout. Because here we are kind of using conditional rendering to add and remove something from our layout, we can trigger those entering and exiting animations. That's what's happening here with this button over here. One of them sets visible to not visible, and this one also sets visible to not visible. One of them is inside this animated view here, so it's going to control this state, and the other one is outside of this toggle control.
Then we have some styles to find at the this is where the X-Bow Snap is more limited than your physical device. I'll show you in a second here what I mean. It doesn't really show animations very well, especially entering and exiting animations. Why? You can kind of control on your physical device quite a bit better.

If you see the purple box and you tap that purple box, you can kind of animate it there or click the button to animate it manually. The rotation red can be here as well; see how it looks. Alright, it's not too bad.

In this case, I am going to take this and I'm going to do the exiting animation, which is looking along the x-axis, and then hit this box here. It will move up, scale down, turn transparent, and then scale back up and return to its original location. This is not going to really give us our full potential animations. The next option we have for custom animations, aside from key framing, which relies on points in time essentially across our animation, is to look at points in space to affect what our animation looks like.

To do this, we use coordinate-based animations and we use something called values, which is going to be a prop that we receive in our custom animations. This gives us access to different locations on the screen, including the parent coordinate system, so like a container that a view exists within. We can get the x and y coordinates of it. We can get the width and height, border radius, origin x and y, and there are lots of other things that are stored inside value that we can use to animate something within a space.

Why might this be useful? Well, you can use this for a number of reasons. We can make this work so that our animations are more responsive, so rather than moving a certain number of pixels or something like that, we can use the x and y locations of the container to constrain an animation's location. We maybe know the overall motivational behavior, so we don't need to rely on keyframes, but we care more about how it interacts with maybe the borders of a container, so it maybe bounces across something. It depends what your overall goal is with your custom animation.

With these coordinate-based animations, we can create a custom animation like this. In this case, we're calling our function custom animation, and it receives these values. These values are where we can receive out-of-order in our animation. In our previous lecture, we weren't explicitly using the worklet directive in most of the examples that we saw, because we were using the use animation state that was automatically converting things into worklets on its own anyway. In this case, because we're defining our own custom function, we have to explicitly say that we work with it, otherwise it might take place on our JavaScript thread, which is going to cause a number of problems, like reducing shared values here, or if we're doing something more computationally expensive, which we don't want to do in our JavaScript thread. So this custom animation that we're defining needs to have three aspects to it, or at least two, one of those optional.
1. The first thing that we want to return back from our custom animation is our animations. A constant called animations where we define all of those transformations and everything else we want to do.
2. The next thing is going to be initial values. This is going to represent where our animation starts. It moves through these animations and it ends up where those animations take it.
3. Then what we have is an optional callback, which is going to take place when our animation completes. The purpose of this is that when the animation reaches its end, then we perform some other behaviors. For example, think about really common apps that you interact with yourself where you have this loading screen animation. Once that loading screen animation ends, maybe you have an animated transition into your first layout or something like that, or you see the pop-up here asking for permissions or something. If you want some behavior to wait for your animation to complete, you can define this callback here, which while the not necessarily a return, but just return initial values and animations.

We also briefly talked about the live site of gestures talking about these six states that they can have going from undefined which means that we don't currently detect any gestures failed which means that gesture was started but didn't complete successfully began meaning that a touch was detected but at this point we're not yet sure which gesture belongs to. Canceled which means that gesture started but it was cancelled for example by moving the pointer off screen active which comes after began where not only did a gesture start but we were also able to detect which gesture was the started and end meaning that the gesture is not ending which could include fail, cancel or a successful end.
