1. The state change event which indicates changes and states. Where should go into the begin state? Sorry, on begin event when we go into the active state, we have the on start event. When we have the end state, we have the on end event. And then on finalized captures end fail or cancel.
2. Not related to any changes in state, it's on update which means that while we're in the active state, there are still changes happening that are not related to the state itself but perhaps it's like the location of pointer that's changing or the number of pointers or fingers that are currently on the screen. Or maybe it's the amount of rotation in a rotate gesture.
3. Pointer events. These ones don't relate to how a state is changed. But simply what is on the screen and what information can we get better? So these will be like raw touch events for example if a finger touches the screen you have on pointer down, if it moves you have on pointer move, on pointer up is when we lift up, and then on pointer cancel is when it moves off the screen. We don't have to be in any sort of gesture state. It doesn't rely on us having entered on begin, on start, on end, or on finalize. So this could be useful if you don't necessarily wanna catch a gesture but more fine-grained touch events.

Any questions about these? These are also listed on the same page that we were just looking at with the gesture events. So you can look at this documentation and see what the different kinds of data are that you can work with for each of these states as well as events on the same screen.

Okay. So in order to use gestures at all, we need something called the GestureHandler GroupView. The GestureHandler GroupView is like the highest level thing in our program that's managing all sorts of gesture information and touch input, everything that comes in. It's the entry point, it's going to interact with all components. So, this would be in our app.js file among the components that are being returned. Our GestureHandler GroupView is going to be wrapping all of those.

The reason for this is that the gesture handler group view is basically what decides where touch input goes. So this is what is deciding whether our gesture input is being centered on gesture or centered on touch. One portion of the screen or another, which gesture gets detected first, or whether it gets sent to a button vs a view that's detecting a gesture - there's a lot of different ways that our touch events could be registered, or even if they're being managed by the operating system (which is our app). There are a lot of different ways they can be registered in Dublin. The GestureHandler review is what's responsible for managing all of that. And so it needs to wrap everything as well.

Within the GestureHandler review, we have something called gesture detector. The gesture detector is responsible for one specific gesture. So we can have multiple gesture detectors within our GestureHandler review. Gesture detector must wrap off components where particular gestures may be detected. So for example, this portion of the screen that might detect a pan gesture, then I'll have the gesture detector with the pan gesture wrapping those components on the screen, and same thing for any other gestures that I want to have. So the gesture detector gives us the ability to recognize, create, and update different gestures. These could be the built-in ones like zoom, blink, pan, finish tap, etc., or the custom ones we create when we talk more about creating custom gestures in future. So this goes beyond just one gesture handler, so you can have multiple gesture handlers within a gesture detector as it allows gesture composition. This might make a little bit more sense in a few slides.

So this is kind of building on the idea of when we have a single platform development, we have gesture handlers that handle one gesture at a time, and Gesture Detectors combine that functionality to go a little bit beyond detecting one gesture at a time, but still it is responsible for either one gesture or one composition of gestures at a time. Hopefully that'll make a little bit more sense once we talk about combining gestures together.

Any questions about this? So this was a pretty simple example. We had one gesture being detected at the time, and I was kind of changing in a clunky way where I was going into that gesture detector and changing it one by one and saving it to see it update. So this is obviously not how we want it to happen in the real world when we have our app support. And sometimes we don't necessarily want to separate it into different parts of the screen and detecting different gestures. We really want different gestures to work together. For example, you can think of this as a map application where you have pinching, rotating, zooming, panning, all that happening together. And we need to be able to deal with the fact that gestures could happen either together or at least in the same area where we want multiple gestures to be able to be detected. This is what we call gesture composition when we can combine gestures, into one detector. With three approaches to composition, these are the brace, simultaneous, and exclusive approaches and they each handle it a little bit differently. So the brace approach is the most straightforward. What this one does is it takes in a number of gestures and they're kind of bracing to be detected. So if I pass in here that gesture dot brace and I have pinch rotate and pan what this is going to do is the gesture here called brace that I pass into a gesture detector is going to be looking for all three gestures but specifically whichever one happens first. So only one can activate at a time and it will be depending on whichever one happens first and the others will be cancelled. So if I have like my two fingers on the screen and I kind of pinch a little bit before I rotate then pinch is going to be detected and rotate and pan are both going to be cancelled. So kind of bracing for detection but all of them can be detected by the same gesture detector. Does that make sense? On the other hand, the simultaneous approach it gives us the ability to do multiple at the same time so they're not bracing for detection they can all be detected at the same time they can all move into the active state simultaneously. So here we have gestures all simultaneous and we're passing in each of these gestures and so this can mean that we get kind of a mix of different print statements in this example because you know we're printing things in like you know our on begin on start on finalize on update events. So we're gonna get a bunch of different things happening at the same time because they're moving through these states simultaneously or at least there's some overlap so none of them are going to cancel each other so this would be the best one to use in like that math example where we want multiple gestures to activate at the same time. Any questions about that? So the third approach is exclusive. This one's pretty similar to race. So this one is going to mean that of the gestures that we provide, only one of them can be active but it handles it a little bit differently. So with race, when one of them becomes active, the others immediately cancel and only the first one that activates is going to move into the detected state. Exclusive puts them in kind of a wait list. So the first one to be activated is going to be detected and the others are going to put on hold until our activated gesture completes. So if our activated gesture completes then we will move on to the next thing in the wait list and if that one activates then it activates and if it doesn't it gets cancelled and the next one is going to be on the wait list and if that one activates then great if it doesn't that one gets cancelled or fails as well. So it's just kind of a matter of whether they immediately become cancelled or if they become cancelled after the first one that was detected or if they actually activate after the first one is detected depending on how the touch goes. So they're sort of being given priority here in comparison to the race approach which is just going to be immediately get rid of all the other gestures. Does this make sense? Any questions about this? So let's take a look at an example of composing our gestures together. So this is going to be the gesture composition demo.  So there's our pen gesture. In this case, it just had to be onStart, onUpdate, onFinalize printing. So it'll print Velocity again for pen. Then the Pinch where we're printing in our pointers again when it updates. And what about exclusive? Just one But how is that different from the race condition? The race is trying to get multiple inputs, but it's picking the fastest one. Whereas the other one is just exclusively... So the difference is, well the book is looking at the fastest one, but race is going to immediately cancel the others. Whereas exclusive is going to keep them on a waitlist until the fastest one completes and then it'll move on to the second one and then that one completes and moves on to the third one So it's one at a time whereas Race is just one. Let me think You might make a mistake to double tap it so single tap it and then it can become a problem. Yeah exactly! What if you double tap it other than just two single taps? Right? So how does our gesture detector know whether it is a double tap being detected for the inner component or two single taps being detected for the outer component which are both within the same area? and they're both the same thing, really. What if we had it the other way around? What if we had an inner component that would detect a single tap and an outer component that would detect a double tap? Wouldn't that be a problem? A regulation, a conflict. Sorry? Two-tap and three-tap have a regulation conflict. It could also be a conflict? Yeah. Yeah, it could. Although in that case it's a little bit less likely to become a conflict because if our inner component is detecting a single tap right we have to tap and then release so it's unlikely to kind of trip up the outer detector as much as this case where um you know our double tap is still looking for the outer adjustment detectors while it's looking for double taps that fit with some of your single tasks so it's like less likely that a single tap will be mistaken for a double tap and more likely than double tap hoping is I guess before I go into the solution here to a problem like this, does anyone have any guesses for how we might handle something like this? This is why we have cross-component interaction. This is one type of cross-component interaction. There are other types of interactions as well. We have some approaches where we can handle this. One of these is the requireExternalGestureToFail method.

So, what this is going to do is it's going to look at a particular gesture and it's going to say, "In order for this one to activate, I require that one to fail first." So, this is where we're defining these gestures. The first one is innerTap, where we're setting it equal to gesture.Tap. And then, we have the number of taps as a kind of method there. So, we have gesture.Tap.number of taps, and we take in the number of taps that are required in this case, too. We're going to require double tap in order to detect this gesture, and then, we have our onStart event, where we're logging the console innerTap.

Then, we're defining our outerTapGesture in a very similar way, except, this time, we're not passing a number of taps, so, by default, it'll just be expecting one tap. When we have our onStart event, where we're logging the console as well. Then, in the same outerTapGesture, we're ending it off with.requireExternalGestureToFail, and we're passing in innerTap.

What this is going to do is it's going to look first at innerTap. If innerTap enters its fail state (meaning you touched the screen when you wanted onBegin, you tapped the screen. You moved on to onStart. You didn't tap the screen a second time, so innerTap went into a fail state. It didn't end successfully because it didn't get two taps), once it has failed, we've taken it into that one tap. We're going to look at outertap and see whether it qualifies for outertap, because we also moved into onBegin and then onStart for outertap.

So, while innerTap is going to fail, our outertap is still active. We're looking at whether innerTap has failed, and our outer tap can finish successfully. So, moves into the end state. Does that make sense? The touch is satisfying the active state for both inner tap and outer tap. Once the inner tap fails, we know we can move into the outer tap. This is one approach. It's pretty straightforward.

Then, we have the simultaneous external gesture method here. So, this one is again going to look at another gesture. Now, we're defining innerTap and outerTap in the same ways, except, this time, we're ending our top with a dot simultaneously external gesture, and we're passing an innerTap.

What this is going to do is it's going to allow that touch to be sent to these two different gestures simultaneously, and they can move through their states simultaneously as well. So, what this will mean is that when you tap the screen for the first time, you can move into onBegin and onStart with both gestures. And then, we go through two different states of these simultaneously. For innerTap, if it taps twice, it's going to detect as innerTap, and simultaneously, it'll detect as two outer taps. This is both two single taps and one double tap. So, the second one is simultaneously, and it'll move into success codes for both. This is different than our composition simultaneous. Here we're looking at the interaction between two gestures. They're both kind of moving through their states. We're basically looking at how the states are being moved through by the two different gestures that are both being activated.

Whereas the composition simultaneous is looking at whether we can even activate two different gestures at the same time. So one of them is we're combining multiple gestures together, and one of them is saying, for this area of the screen, where we're looking at two separate gestures, which one is going to activate? Hopefully that makes sense.

One of them is kind of looking at dividing up touch events into these different gesture detectors, and one is passing them over into the same gesture detector and looking at which one can actually activate.

Another approach we have here to gesture interactions is to block external gesture. Here we're defining our innerTap again in our outerTap, except for ending our outerTap with ".blocksExternalGesture" or passing it innerTap.

What this is going to do is basically the same thing as requireExternalGestureToFail but in the opposite direction. So this time instead of saying we require innerTap to fail first before outerTap can complete, what we're saying is we're going to block innerTap entirely until outerTap first moves to all its states and then once because you've blocked innerTap, outerTap can complete first. Then innerTap can occur only if outerTap fails. So in this case, innerTap is going to require innerTap to fail first before it successfully completes.

So you can decide with the amount of screen space that you have whether one element is going to take a certain amount more space or another and this is all flexible essentially so it will move to a larger screen with the size as well without overflowing off the screen. So the way that we could use flex blocks is first we have to define a parent which is going to be our container and this container sets the bounds of where we want to control our elements within. Within this container we give it flex items which are going to be the ones that are going to be the children of this parent component or parent element and these are the items that are going to be given flex values or relative weights that will affect how they are assigned space. Then using the flex box properties for each of those flex items we can control things like their their side their spacing and then with the flex container we can apply things like orientation alignment which are going to affect those child elements。Now we could also change this even further so to give this a relative flex of 3 and this would take up even more space or we could give things you know we could adjust the flex values that we give them to assign them more or less space relative to one another. Any questions? Then we also have flex-shrink and flex-grow. These are similar to flex but they kind of just work in three different directions. So flex-grow gives us a value towards which our item can grow. Flex-shrink gives us a value towards which our item can shrink. These are between zero and one but they work very similarly to flex.

And then we have justify-content which compared to align-items (that goes on the cross axis), JustifyContent works on the main axis. And then same with the other options that are there. Any questions about the options that you can use for sub-stops? And you'll probably find a lot of this pretty familiar from your previous work with CSS in past courses, but there are some differences.
So just a summary of what I mentioned:
- The defaults are different
- Flex direction defaults here to column instead of to row, which it would do in CSS
- Align content defaults to start instead of stretch, which it would in CSS
- Flex string defaults to 0 instead of 1
- Flex only supports a single number
In CSS, you would be able to give it several numbers which would represent flex, flexGrow, and flexShrink. Here they are separated out into different values that you have to define individually. So the flex property is what assigns the different amount of fill or how much of the space that the variable will take. So it's grown quite a bit. Started off as just integrating applications that allowed for some GPS tracking, so primarily those would have been Foursquare and Facebook. But I think that expectation of integration has definitely grown into applications that don't necessarily do any GPS tracking but more sharing files, not necessarily with other people. So while they could be collaborative applications, a lot of the times is you integrating different apps so that you can have access to your information in different devices that you yourself use. Another feature, again, that most of you will likely use in your projects will be the use of APIs. One very common use of an API are payment systems. I don't know if you remember, but I remember pre-COVID going to get a coffee and seeing a big ginormous POS system at the coffee shop, almost as big as the big cappuccino machine. After COVID hit, now we go to a coffee shop and they have a little square – some of them have the little tablet with a little square pouch. If you go to the farmer's market, right, the farmer's market before COVID, probably a few years before COVID, you had to go with cash; nobody took cards. Now, if you go to the farmer's market, most of them (not all of them) will have the little square tab, or if you don't see the square tab, they'll have the app on their phone so that you can use the NFC payment option on their phone. That's an API. If they don't have Square, they'll have Clover, or if they don't have Clover, they'll have Stripe, but they all do the same thing. While they did start as a much more approachable POS system for small businesses, allowing for car payments is quite expensive for small businesses. So, while it did allow for more affordable ways of allowing that kind of payment, they very quickly started to expand their features to better invoice tracking, better tax reporting, and better expense reporting as well. Allowing the user to not only use this as a POS system just for taking payments, but now getting a more holistic view of their business income and expenses. Specifically for small businesses. And we see that, like, more and more, it's not only for payments, but we do see APIs, particularly in that department, being used quite a bit. In terms of why we get to see users, if we think about it from the perspective of small businesses, why would they use any APIs? They are, of course, not wondering about the development part; they wonder more about the experience from their end and their users. It's just going back to that first topic, facilitating a task, facilitating the payment part, right, creating a much easier experience with that. You can have the receipt sent to your email, which I think is awesome because I always hated receipts. As far as we're concerned as developers, it allows us to work a little bit better with data because it allows us to make that data that we might be working with available to a different application, available in a different service, or even a different module within our application a little bit more easily. And also provide asynchronous data to our website or another website. And it also allows us to set SOA principles and RESTful development practices from the very beginning.

It kind of forces us to, because most of us, when we start to consider that we want to work with a particular API, and it might just be me (I tend to particularly if it's an API that I've never used before), I will go through the documentation, trying to map out any considerations that it sets out that the developer has to do. So if I have to code in a particular way or if I have to create a particular service in order to interact with something, anything that it tells me "you have to do things this way in order to be able to use our API", I start trying to map them out (just not necessarily coding them, but just mapping them out) so that if any of those practices are different from how I usually approach application development, then I can better visualize what I have to do.

I tend to start from the very beginning, setting up the structure of how I need to code this application considering the API I'm going to be using from the very beginning, as opposed to what I used to do (develop the application the normal way that I normally would) and then only the part where I'm dealing with the API take into consideration whatever those requirements might be. That was not very nice when it came to testing for me, because it did force me to have to switch how I thought about things differently. And there was always a part of me that wanted to fix, even though I didn't need fixing, just fix and get everything to follow the same thought process.

So this way just makes it easier, because then I see that the structure of what I created matches regardless of whether it is following because it has to follow requirements from the API, or if it is just following the requirements, even though it doesn't have to follow in a particular part.

so that the user gets the information relatively fast yes Week4.These are the aspects of information design:
- Site maps for information organization, storage, and production design
- Wireframes for information and navigation design
- Prototypes for interface design and other aspects of the project
- Visual design for our actual implementation level fidelity of our application
We'll dive into these steps. Some people like to organize this in this way. This is not the only way to design your application! But it's common to start with a user journey map, and then we go into wireframes. Let's know what the flow is to the application, kind of mocking up some of those screens in a very rough way. Then create a critical conflict that's not so functional but it helps to understand how that flow will be incorporated into those wireframes, and then move onto the visual design. This is one process. It's not necessarily the process I recommend or the one that fits best with this project, but it's one to keep in mind.
So the next part of this lecture we're going to look at is these actual tools that we're talking about and in particular how they might relate to your project. What are things that have come across use cases before in your previous classes? Quite a few of these. This is going to be similar to how you would create a use case when you're doing software design, for example, where you have a set of actors and you have some form for those actors to achieve their goals, and they might be communicating through the system, or interacting with the system or one another in different ways.
For example, in this case we have a use case design where a waiter, a client, a cashier, and a chef are the actors in the system, and the system is the restaurant in this case. So, the client might order food and it's supposed to interact with the waiter who receives the order and the chef who has to confirm the order. And so you can have these little main bowls or spud bowls that each of you can use. This is kind of a software designed look at what a use case is, but at a high level, the use case is going to be like: who is going to be using your application? Where is your data coming from? And what are your databases? Who is going to be interacting with the user? For example, we've had multiple kinds of users who interact with one another, each of them with different cases. So, I know there's some cases in other projects where you have let's say a back-end for the client to manage information from customers. So, now you have two different types of users who are going to be interacting with each other, each with slightly different goals. Each of their goals are. Who are they? And how does the system manage their interactions with one another? You don't necessarily need to guide them with each project. This is just to tie this concept in with the other concept in the library.

The next thing that is useful here is a sitemap! A sitemap isn't really as related to user experience. This has more to do with how you navigate your application and how the information is organized. So, this shows the different views or screens within your application and the different functionalities that are going to be present on your screen, for the different types of content that are going to be present on your screen.

So, for example, if you start with the main screen, and from there you have three different screens to go through: Activity One, Activity Two, and Activity Three. Now, these might have subsections within each of these screens that you can then display like this as a child note in a sitemap, or potentially those are going to these screens themselves that are linked to other screens, and then you would have a continuing structure within your sitemap to get deeper and deeper.

Another way to display it is like what you see on the bottom here. This is an example of an Outlook Mail app. So, say you're displaying the screen for accounts, and then on there you have three accounts that the user has: a Gmail, a Dell, and an FBS email. Within each of those, you have series of folders, and then within each folder, you have some messages. Each one may be for a different sender, and then within each of those messages, you have the title and some content. So, you could display it as kind of a linked structure where you go from one activity to the next.

Any questions about this? No more than five levels of depth. That basically means no more than five screens that link to scenes and then between those screens, because at this point in the page, it's easy to get lost from hierarchy to one screen and then go back, and that keeps it more easy to navigate.
This has to be balanced between low branching, so you can't have both necessarily low branching and shallow, but you want to have a limited number of items per view, so it will be like fewer children per each view. This means that you don't necessarily want to display 8 different functionalities on one stream because that's also confusing.
If it is balanced, do I have more streams that link to screens that link to streams, or do I have more content on each screen? And there's not exactly a perfect balance that you can strike. This is going to come down to what those functionalities are. If you do need to have the hierarchy, then you want to make sure that they are made for multiple views. In many cases, low branching is not possible, and shallowness is usually more important. But ideally, you want to strike a balance here. And you wanna have clear labels on all children in any sitemap that you create, both in your sitemap and in general, clearly listed throughout the application as well.
So here's another example of a sitemap. So here we have our home screen, and then from our home screen, we have 5 different screens that you can go to:
1. Log events
2. About us
3. Join us as sponsors
4. Tutorials
5. Lifestyle posts
And then from Claude you can go to tutorials, lifestyle posts, from events you can go to event calendar, attendance guidelines, etc.
So you can see our deepest branch here goes 4 levels in, so you'll move up to History, to Mission, to Press. But each of these are on one level, so it's not like history leads to mission and mission leads to press, so that would be a different level of depth. So really here we have 2 levels.
Sitemaps are good for understanding information hierarchy and structure, but we can also use storyboards. The role of storyboards is quite different. So rather than showing how information is divided across these different screens, storyboards show how those use cases actually work. So they show the functional details of each of the blocks in the hierarchy and an accompanying description of each case with the description. This should give you a strong grasp of user actions, but specifically, the flow in those user actions. You know that you have an about us page and a blog page. That's communicated by format, but what's not communicated is how you would go for a particular use case, let's say the use case is finding a specific piece of information about an article in 2015. That could be a use case. Now, for that particular use case, how would you navigate from one screen to another or to access all the different aspects of that book that you want to? This is what you want to do on a storyboard, so it's that sense of flow and how you achieve each action for each actor for each use case.The first way to identify common use cases is to make use easier by creating shortcuts.
Once you have one clear use case, usually there are ways to combine them, but for clarity it's useful to separate them. You can identify unexpected use cases which can affect functionality.
As you're going through these different flows, you might discover things you didn't expect, so this is another way to do this. You can also identify un-missed use cases. As you're going through, you might notice one of these screens hasn't been used at all in this storyboard. Is it unnecessary and do you cut it out? Or is there a missed use case that we didn't mention on the list but we always intend on having?
Storyboards used to be called clickstreams. They sometimes actually still are, and it describes the use case of typical usage scenario. It does not describe a future or the history of a click that you go through to accomplish specific uses.
Any questions about storyboards so far?
This is an example of a storyboard. This is quite simple. In this case, you are going through a click-and-click button on the first screen, it opens up the second screen. On that second screen, you see a few options:
1. Going to sign in
2. You click sign in and it takes you to the next screen
3. On that next screen, you have a search button that takes you to a search screen
So you can actually kind of see the flow of how these different clicks will take you to different screens. In this case, it's quite simple, you really only have one or two arrows going to each area, but it can get even clearer than this!
I would recommend annotating this screen for as much as possible. For each of those arrows, creating annotations for what that action is, as part of the use case, that the actor is trying to accomplish.
This is an example of combining multiple use cases into one storyboard. The curl here, like when I would look at this storyboard and think it was great that it was empty because now I can understand what actions are actually being completed by each of these streams or flows within the story board.
So that part is great! It's not so great because it's a little bit confusing to separate out the different use cases from one another here. You have kind of like multiple arrows going between screens, so maybe like on the centre screen over there you have multiple button clicks and you can see where each of those button clicks will take the user next. So it's pretty good for display in that one stream, but I now can't tell which arrow relates to what.
That's part of the reason why I recommend separating out different storyboards for your different use cases if possible. Any questions about storyboards?
The questions you want to ask at this stage are:
- Are users doing what they intended or are they using the software differently?
- For example, is their understanding of them was wrong?
So if the use case that I'm giving someone is to place an order from this application and I've created a storyboard and I started it out of a home page, maybe I expected them first to go into like browse or something like that. But when I show the storyboard to my friend, they actually go straight to their cart first. Now that would show that their thinking was a little bit different than what I expected their thinking to be, and maybe the storyboard I created is not going to be successful in actually demonstrating how users accomplish this action. Does that make sense? Don't match the application to the user behavior. Just because the user does something, it doesn't mean the user wants to do it that way. This is very important, especially as you're talking to your clients and they're going to be giving you ideas about how they can make something go better. The user can tell you one thing really well: they can tell you a problem. They can't tell you a solution. That's really bad! So they can show you the problem, for example, if they don't follow the clickstream that you expected. That can tell you a problem. Maybe the problem is that your visual design is not clear. Maybe your information architecture is not clear. Maybe they couldn't see the button that you expected them to click or the higher structure buttons that are more evident in the first. How do I identify this problem? Now you, as a designer and developer, come up with this solution: do I make this button that I expect them to use more clear? Or am I actually misunderstanding their goal in the first place? Now if you're misunderstanding their goal in the first place, that's a sign that you should go back a step and be able to try to use this solution. But you don't necessarily want to match something to user behavior because it's probably a symptom of the actual cause rather than their end solution. Does that two different purposes entirely.

The sitemap can tell us what the views are of the functionalities and pieces of the application, how they're structured relative to one another. This can also give us a sense of the initial design of the view. In particular, it tells us where does the content and functionalities show up on this different screen? It's basically a static view of what the system has. So it just tells you in total what's included as part of its system and the views and the foundation of sitemap.

The story board, on the other hand, will tell you the flow of usage between those different pieces that are identified in sitemap. It will tell you the initial design of the web for user's experience. Not just where is the information and how is it structured, but how does the user interact with that information? It's a more dynamic view of how a system is used and events and activities (essentially use cases).

So you don't necessarily need to develop these separately. You can, and it would be a good idea, but if you use an augmented sitemap, the two can be created at the same time. What I mean by this is if your sitemap shows visual components, for example, and you can show a flow between them, there's a way to show that structure as part of the story board as well. This all kind of comes down to how you structure it. I don't have an example of this to show, but you can kind of imagine that instead of that original sitemap, you would have sketches of the different screens and then you could have arrows between them while still maintaining the hierarchy and high-resolution.

The next thing that is going to be useful to you and that you'll be creating is a wireframe. A wireframe is a UI-independent rendition of this content layout. Basically, a sketch of what your system is going to look like. When I say UI-independent, I mean that colors, fonts, how things look doesn't matter. You can have placeholders for a lot of things. The point is to understand the layout and basically a very low-fidelity look at what the application is going to look like. It's sort of a back-of-the-envelope or a cocktail-at-level of detail. It's just the general idea.

It's actually not better to have more detail at this stage. It's easy to think that this is a lazy version of your screen and that to go above and beyond you add more detail. This is not the case! If you add more detail at this stage, you're narrowing your thinking in future stages because you're becoming tied to a particular UI design that was your initial idea, and now because all these different variations in the wireframe, you might think it has to show up in the final product.

I recommend at this point not defining these things clearly because it will give you more scope for improvement later on. The wireframes used to answer questions like: How do we lay out the foundation elements? What visuals, interactions or voice should we use? What are the best ways to present information? Metaphors here can take on the driving elements of a lot of interaction design. For example, do you remember from a few lectures ago we were talking about skeuomorphism and how real-life things can become metaphors for one another? For example, with an office system, like a desktop system. It's the metaphor of an office that has folders and files and everything is present on the desktop. This is a metaphor that drives the information layer and interactions.

There are other metaphors as well that you might be using to create these wireframes. So at this point don't worry about UI details. In fact, stay away from UI details. Use annotations to describe interactions. For example, if you have a block that is known to be an interactive video slider thing, you can just block it off and say: "Interactive Video Slider." And you don't have to show any details beyond that.

At this stage, you can get feedback from others. So you have, again, very locally, a prototype of what your application looks like. Show it to your friends, see how they interact with it and how they understand the initial layout. And again, think about whether their feedback is a symptom of the problems that your application has. You also want to iterate through several different layouts. The more iterations, the better you'll find the product results. You don't want to just prove that your initial layout was good because this initial idea at the end was like PowerPoint, any Adobe Creative Suite tool. Paint. That's updated, but you can basically use any tool that you want. I recommend going with something quick and easy. You don't want to spend a lot of time on this. This brings us to prototypes. So, prototypes are kind of initial versions of what your end results are going to look like. So, you have different pedalities of prototypes because you will keep iterating on these projects and making them better and better. So, the point of prototypes is to get users to see your design and interact with your design and give you some feedback on the design as you go through these different stages in order to find better versions that you can iterate on and continually improve your application. At this point, for example, right now we're probably looking at low fidelity or very very low fidelity prototypes of our application using a larger model. It's good to start with these low fidelity ones and it's good to start with cheap or bad looking prototypes at this stage. The reason for this is sort of counterintuitive but if I were to show a user a really great interactive game that comes with a good application are they going to be honest with me when they see that? Probably not because they'll see that I've already put a lot of time into this and they'll think it's already pretty late into the development stage and they might think there's not really a lot of room left for improvement. Now if I show them sketching back in the napkin they're much more likely to give me real feedback about how I'm doing this. So you can make these low fidelity prototypes literally sketches on paper or something like that in this example. They're using these paper printouts of what a mobile screen looks like and getting the user to interact with them literally by tapping paper and then swapping in other paper screens as the app we gave similar to a storyboard that can interact with them. This is a great way to test out your applications before spending all your time developing new ones. I'm going through this stuff pretty quickly so I won't waste time at the end but high fidelity prototypes are going to be computer-based prototypes that are high functionality, this could even be your final version of the app or very close to it but you're still not familiar to the design yet and you want to leave some room for improvement if you're planning to show it to users and get feedback. So essentially the idea of this is that you can be closer and closer to your final design while still leaving room for improvement at each stage. The next thing I want to go over is LashList, this is going to be useful for your labs as well. The example we're going to look at is one that you're going to build on for your next lab. So we have two different ways of displaying lists on mobile screens, lists on mobile screens that will probably be useful. You'll probably all end up using them for your projects. For formatting lots of information. This is not necessarily just text that's being listed out, it could be formatting content with images, and you know, certain types of grids and things that you need to display, like data sets on one screen.
So the different ways that we could display this would be:
1. Scrollboot, which is a scrolling container that renders the entire list of elements whether they're on screen or not. That's a spoiler for the fact that PlotList does not behave that way, so PlotList will only render items on the screen which helps it through that performance in the list.
2. PlotList, which will only render items on the screen. This helps it through that performance in the list. What this means is that say you have 200 elements in a list or in a database and you want to now display those in a list. Your screen can probably only fit five of those elements at a time. If we render all 200 of them, we're using up valuable resources. And that's going to slow down PlotList's rendering process. And rendering the file that you can actually see on your screen or as you scroll adding new ones to your rendering we're saving some of these resources. So that's beneficial for performance.
3. Section List. This one is similar to PlatList, so it has the same benefits, but it's specifically for when you have subcategories of benefits. So you might want to use section lists for specific types of lists that you want to select. Like when you put your finger on the screen and you move it up rather than simply scrolling as much as you move your finger it will continue scrolling in a smooth way.
So, it seems simple but not all groups have these lists because how does that come together? So this can be helpful, for example, if you have a large database and you want to scroll from 1 to 200 pretty quickly, it's a lot faster to do this.

Imagine you have a super helper at school — not a real person, but a smart robot brain called AI.
Today I want to tell you how grown-ups use this “smart helper” at work, and why it’s good, and sometimes not so good.

First, what does this “smart helper” do?
Think of AI like a friendly helper who can look at lots of information super fast. For example:
	•	It can help doctors read pictures of the inside of your body, like X-rays.
	•	It can help teachers check homework quickly.
	•	It can help people at stores keep track of what things are running out.
	•	It can help drivers stay safe by warning them if a car is too close.

It’s kind of like having a super fast classmate who loves doing big, boring tasks so people can focus on the fun or important parts.

Now, what’s good about this helper?

First, it never gets tired. AI can work all day and night.
Second, it doesn’t forget things like we do.
Third, it helps grown-ups finish their work faster, so they can go home earlier or do more creative things.

But there are some “not-so-good” parts too.

Sometimes the helper makes mistakes, especially if the information it learned from wasn’t correct.
Sometimes it doesn’t understand feelings — if someone is sad or scared, AI can’t always tell.
And people still need to check its work, because even a smart robot brain can get things wrong.

So the best way to use AI is to treat it like a helper, not a boss. Grown-ups still make the final decisions.

In short:
AI is like a super helper that can do big tasks fast.
It makes work easier, but we still need smart people to guide it.

Thanks for listening! If you ever build your own robot helper one day, I hope it’s kind and cool.
